Faith and Disbelief

4 min
The Logical Dilemma of Coercion to Blasphemy
Baqillani presents a dilemma about coercion to blasphemy and refutes two positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma in coercion to blasphemy and how Baqillani refutes both extreme positions.

Core Idea
A dilemma proves neither extreme coercion position is rationally consistent.

Imam Baqillani presents a classic dilemma: what must a person do when threatened with death unless they utter blasphemy? He considers two possible answers—commit blasphemy or refuse even at the cost of life—and shows both lead to absurd consequences. This lesson traces his reasoning and exposes the logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma scenario
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the scenario: a person fears death if he does not blaspheme but hopes for life if he does. Ponder the question: 'What is incumbent upon him?'
Notice the tension between survival and religious duty.
2
Identify the two opposing claims
Look for two claims: first, that he must commit blasphemy; second, that he must never disbelieve, even at the cost of his life. These are the two extremes Baqillani refutes.
3
Understand Baqillani's refutation of each
For the first claim, Baqillani argues it makes a prohibited act (blasphemy) permissible, which is a contradiction. For the second, he argues it makes self-destruction permissible after it was prohibited, also a contradiction.
Both refutations rely on the principle that rational prohibitions cannot be reversed arbitrarily.
4
Appreciate the broader principle
Realize that Baqillani is not offering a resolution but exposing the inconsistency of his opponents' logic. The lesson teaches that moral reasoning must be coherent.
This dilemma is a tool for theological argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُسيء الثناء عليه وخاف نزول القتل به إن لم يَفْعَلْ كَلِمَة الكفر وشتم ربّ العالمين ورجاء البقاء والحياة إن فعله ما الذي يجب عليه؟ فإن قالوا : يجب عليه فِعْلُ شَتْم ربّ العالمين وسُوءِ الثناء عليه، يقال لهم : فقـد صـار المحظور في العقل مباحاً وكذلك إن قالوا يلزمه ألا يَكْفُر وإن أدى ذلك إلى تلف نفسه، قيل لهم : فقد صار قتل نفسه وإلقاؤها في التَّهلُكَةِ مباحاً بعد أن كان محظوراً وهذا ما كرهتم المصير إليه .
He speaks ill of Him and fears that killing will befall him if he does not utter the word of disbelief and abuse the Lord of the worlds, and hopes for survival and life if he does it. What is incumbent upon him? If they say: He must abuse the Lord of the worlds and speak ill of Him, it is said to them: Then the prohibited in the intellect has become permissible. And likewise if they say: He must not disbelieve even if that leads to the destruction of himself, it is said to them: Then the killing of himself and casting himself into destruction has become permissible after it was prohibited, and this is what you disliked to reach.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a moral dilemma and refutes two opposing positions. It begins with a scenario: a person fears death if he does not say a word of disbelief (كلمة الكفر) and abuse God, but hopes for life if he does. The question is what is obligatory (ما الذي يجب عليه). The author then presents two possible claims: first, that he must commit blasphemy; second, that he must not, even at the cost of his life. For the first claim, the author argues that this would make what is rationally prohibited (المحظور) become permissible (مباح), which is a contradiction. For the second claim, the author retorts that it would make suicide permissible after it was prohibited, which is also a contradiction. The conclusion is that the opponents cannot consistently hold either position without undermining their own principles.

Key Terms
كلمة الكفر
محظور
مباح
إلقاء النفس في التهلكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Baqillani is endorsing situational ethics or advocating a middle way, but he is actually exposing logical inconsistency in his opponents' positions.
Correction: Baqillani is not prescribing a solution; he is showing that both extreme positions lead to contradictions within the opponents' own framework.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what happens if one claims that the coerced person must commit blasphemy?
A) He becomes a martyr.
B) The prohibited becomes permissible.
C) He is excused.
D) The act becomes obligatory forever.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that both extreme responses to coercion to blasphemy are rationally untenable because they entail making a prohibited act permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were in that situation—fearing death unless you blaspheme—what would your heart truly rely on? Would your fear of people outweigh your fear of your Lord? This passage strips away rationalization: both choices are inconsistent. Where does your ultimate security lie?
?
Have you ever compromised a principle out of fear of losing something (reputation, job, relationships)? How does Baqillani's argument expose the hidden inconsistency in your own heart's justifications? What would it take for you to trust Allah's promise over the threat of harm?
?
When you weigh two difficult options, do you ever stop to check if both contradict a clear prohibition? This passage forces you to examine whether you are rationalizing sin. Are you willing to accept the logical consequence of your choices, even if it means facing death or loss?

When Uttering Disbelief Is Preferable
Baqillani answers a further objection about which action is preferable under coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that neither uttering nor refraining from the word of disbelief under coercion is categorically preferable; rather, the preference depends on inward belief and the consequences of each action.

Core Idea
Actions under coercion are judged by inward belief and avoidance of greater harm.

The discussion on coercion continues. The objectors have argued that uttering the word of disbelief (فعل كلمة الكفر) is preferable to being killed. Baqillani responds by turning the argument around: he first questions why refraining (الكف) with death is not preferable, since the person avoids abusing God. Then, when the objectors change their stance, he shows that uttering can be preferable if the person's inward belief remains pure and refraining leads to self-destruction. This passage sharpens the moral reasoning behind actions under duress.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage twice
First, read without stopping. Then read again, noticing the two dialectical moves: the objectors' claim and the author's counter, then the objectors' shift and the author's detailed scenario.
The passage uses a series of conditional questions (فما أنكرتم, قيل لهم).
2
Identify the two objections and answers
Find where the objectors first say uttering is preferable, then where they say refraining is preferable. Notice how Baqillani's first answer challenges the premise, and the second answer provides a scenario where uttering is justified.
3
Understand the condition for uttering
In the second answer, focus on the condition: 'when he does not embrace disbelief inwardly' (لم يشرح بالكفر صدراً). This is the key to why uttering can be acceptable.
The author also mentions knowing God's awareness of one's true belief, preserving life, and avoiding destruction of property (إتلاف ملك الرب).
4
Consider the underlying principle
Reflect on the principle: actions under coercion are judged by the agent's inner state and the avoidance of greater harm. Uttering with inward integrity is not a sin, but refraining while causing self-destruction is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم، إن قالوا : فعل كلمة الكفر أولى : فما أنكرتم أن يكون الكف مع القتل أولى لأنه يكف عن شتم ربه وليس هو القاتل لنفسه؟ فإن قالوا : فالكفّ عمـا قلتم أولى ، قيل لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون إظهار كلمة الكفر أولى إذا لم يَشْرَحْ بالكفر صدراً لحفظ نفسه وعِلْمِهِ بأن الله سبحانه عالم باعتقاده وأنه مُخلِص في وحدانيتـه وأنه لا سبحانه بإظهار ما يظهره وأنه هو يَستَضِرُّ بترك إظهاره ويُطَرِّقُ إلى قتل نفسه وتَعَدّي الحق في إتلاف مِلْكِ ربِّه وفعل المحظور عليه فعله ؛ ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك.
And it is said to them: If they say: The act of the word of disbelief is preferable: then why do you not deny that refraining with killing is preferable because he refrains from abusing his Lord and he is not the killer of himself? If they say: Refraining from what you said is preferable, it is said to them: Why do you not deny that manifesting the word of disbelief is preferable when he does not embrace disbelief inwardly for the sake of preserving his life and his knowledge that God, exalted is He, knows his belief and that he is sincere in His oneness and that He [؟] by manifesting what he manifests and that he harms himself by refraining from manifesting it and leads to killing himself and transgressing the truth in destroying the property of his Lord and doing what is prohibited for him to do; and they have no answer for that.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical exchange. The objectors first claim that uttering the word of disbelief (فعل كلمة الكفر) is preferable (أولى) to death. The author counters by asking why refraining (الكف) along with being killed is not preferable, since the person refrains from abusing God and is not the agent of his own death. When the objectors then shift and say that refraining from what the author said is preferable, the author responds by explaining a scenario where uttering the word of disbelief is actually preferable. This is when the person does not inwardly embrace disbelief (لم يشرح بالكفر صدراً), knows that God knows his true belief and sincerity, and realizes that by refraining he would harm himself and lead to his own death, which would be tantamount to destroying the property of his Lord (ملك ربه) and doing what is prohibited. The author asserts that the objectors have no answer to this argument.

Key Terms
فعل كلمة الكفر
الكف
شرح الصدر بالكفر
إتلاف ملك الرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani advocates outward apostasy as always acceptable under coercion.
Correction: Baqillani only permits uttering when the person does not inwardly embrace disbelief (لم يشرح بالكفر صدراً) and does so to preserve life and avoid greater harm, not as a categorical allowance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when might uttering a word of disbelief be preferable to refraining?
A) When the person outwardly embraces disbelief.
B) When the person does not inwardly accept disbelief and knows God knows his true belief.
C) When the person wants to avoid death at all costs.
D) When the person is alone.

Key Takeaway
Under coercion, the preferability of uttering versus refraining depends on inward belief and the consequences for life and property, not on a blanket rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where speaking a word of disbelief could save your life, do you trust that Allah sees your heart and will hold you accountable only for your intention?
?
Are you willing to risk your life to avoid uttering what you know is false, or would you utter it while praying that Allah knows your true belief?
?
If you were under coercion to deny your faith, would you be able to maintain sincere belief in your heart, or would the outward act lead you to doubt? Can you honestly say your faith is that strong?

Analogy of Permissibility: Self-Destruction and Animal Harm
Baqillani refutes an objection using an analogy about harming animals with permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses an analogy about animal harm to clarify that conditional permission does not reverse the original rational prohibition.

Core Idea
Conditional permission does not reverse the original prohibition.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses an objection about the rational permissibility of self-destruction when facing disbelief. The objectors argue that if self-destruction prevents disbelief, it becomes permissible without reversing the prohibition. Baqillani counters with an analogy: just as harming an animal is prohibited unless its owner permits it, so too the prohibition of self-destruction can be lifted conditionally without changing the rational rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a response.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two versions of the objection
Find where the objectors present two alternative scenarios: (1) self-destruction is prohibited unless it prevents disbelief, and (2) disbelief is prohibited unless it leads to self-destruction.
Both are introduced by 'أو قالوا'.
3
Understand the refutation via analogy
Baqillani responds by drawing an analogy to harming an animal. The key point: just as harming an animal is conditionally permitted if the owner allows it, so self-destruction is conditionally permitted if it prevents disbelief.
The analogy preserves the original prohibition while allowing conditional exceptions.
4
Reflect on the conditionality of rational rulings
Consider why the objectors thought the ruling might be reversed. Baqillani shows that conditional permissibility does not overturn the underlying prohibition; it merely applies a condition.
This illustrates a broader principle in rational theology: rulings can be contingent without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن هم قالوا إن إلقاء النفس في التهلكة : محظور في العقل إذا لم يُؤدِّ إلى الكفر بصانعها وجحد نعمه وإن أدى إلى ذلك كان مباحاً ؛ أو قالوا : إن الكفر بالصانع محظور في العقل إذا لم يؤد إلى تلف النفس فإن أدى إليه كان مباحاً من غير أن ينقلب المباح في العقل محظوراً ، قيل لهم : وكذلك إتلاف الحيوان وإيلامه محظور في العقل إن لم يُبحه مالكه فإن أباحه لم يكن محظوراً في العقل من غير إتلافه نفسه بقضية العقول أو كان مشروطاً بما كان شرطاً له.
If they say: Casting oneself into destruction is prohibited in the intellect if it does not lead to disbelief in its Maker and denial of His blessings, and if it leads to that then it is permissible; or they say: Disbelief in the Maker is prohibited in the intellect if it does not lead to the destruction of the self, and if it leads to that then it is permissible without the permissible in the intellect being reversed as prohibited, it is said to them: Likewise, destroying an animal and causing it pain is prohibited in the intellect if its owner does not permit it; if he permits it, then it is not prohibited in the intellect without destroying himself by the ruling of the intellects or it is conditioned by what was its condition.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two related objections. The objectors first claim that self-destruction (إلقاء النفس في التهلكة) is rationally prohibited unless it leads to disbelief, in which case it becomes permissible. Alternatively, they claim that disbelief is prohibited unless it leads to self-destruction, in which case it becomes permissible, and this does not entail a reversal of the rational ruling. The author responds by drawing an analogy with harming an animal (إتلاف الحيوان وإيلامه). Harming an animal is prohibited unless its owner permits it; if the owner permits it, it is no longer prohibited. The author implies that just as permissibility depends on the owner's permission, permissibility in the original case depends on the condition of necessity, without a reversal of the rational ruling. The phrase 'without destroying himself by the ruling of the intellects or being conditioned by what was its condition' suggests that the ruling is conditional, not a reversal.

Key Terms
إلقاء النفس في التهلكة
إتلاف الحيوان
إيلام
مالك
مباح
محظور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author equates self with animal property, implying the self can be owned.
Correction: The author is using a logical analogy to show that conditional permission does not overturn the original prohibition; he is not equating the self with property.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Baqillani use to respond to the objection about self-destruction?
A) Breaking a promise.
B) Harming an animal with its owner's permission.
C) Stealing from a thief.
D) Praying under duress.

Key Takeaway
Conditional permissibility does not entail a reversal of the original rational prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever  justified a sin by telling yourself it prevents a greater sin? What does Baqillani's analogy reveal about the logic of that reasoning—are you actually nullifying the prohibition or merely conditioning it?
?
When you face a moral dilemma where both options seem prohibited, how do you determine which one takes precedence? Does your intellect rely on conditional permissions like the objectors, or do you appeal to a higher principle?
?
If you truly believe that prohibitions remain even when conditionally permitted, how does that change the way you approach a 'necessary' sin? Will you still feel the weight of the original prohibition or treat the act as fully permissible?

Mu'tazila on Rational Obligations Without Divine Command
Baqillani states the Mu'tazila view on rational obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that certain duties are obligatory by reason alone, independent of divine command, and why Baqillani considers this a disgraceful view.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot make something obligatory without God's command.

Baqillani begins his indictment by highlighting a foundational Mu'tazila principle: that many acts, like rational inquiry and gratitude, are obligatory even without a divine command. This view elevates human reason above revelation, which Baqillani sees as a grave error. In this lesson, we examine this claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that Baqillani lists specific examples: reflection, inquiry, gratitude, abandoning disbelief.
2
Identify the core claim
The Mu'tazila assert that many things are obligatory without any command from Allah. Ask yourself: how does this conflict with Ash'ari views on obligation?
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider each example: rational inquiry, gratitude, abandoning disbelief. Why might the Mu'tazila consider these obligatory by reason?
4
Understand Baqillani's criticism
Baqillani calls this a 'disgraceful view' because it undermines the role of revelation. Think about why this is a major point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم قولهم : إن كثيراً من الأشياء تجب على العبد من غير أن يكون من أمر الله تعالى فيه أمر مثل النظر والاستدلال وشكر المنعم، وترك الكفر والكفران . ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم قولهم : إن العبد لا يحصل له صفة الإيمان حتى يعلم جميع ما هو شرط في اعتقادهم ويبلغ في معرفته درجة علمائهم كأبي هذيل والنظام وغيرهما، ويقدر فيه على تقرير الدلالة ويتمكن من المناظرة ،والمجادلة، ومن لم يبلغ تلك الدرجة كان كافراً لا يحكم له بالإيمان ولهذا حكموا بالكفر على جميع عوام المسلمين ومما زادوا على هذا ما هو أفضح منه فأنكروا من مفاخر رسول الله ما كان مختصاً به زائداً على الأنبيـاء كـوجـود المعراج، وثبوت الشفاعة له يوم القيامة ووجود حوض الكوثر وأنكروا ما ورد في هذه الأبواب الآثار والأخبار وأنكروا عذاب القبر أيضاً وما في ذلك من نصوص قرآنية.
And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed many things are obligatory upon the servant without there being any command from Allah, exalted, concerning them, such as reflection, rational inquiry, gratitude to the Benefactor, and abandoning disbelief and ingratitude. And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed the servant does not attain the quality of faith until he knows all that is a condition in their belief and reaches in his knowledge the level of their scholars like Abu Hudhayl, al-Nazzam, and others, and becomes able to establish the proof and capable of debate and argumentation; and whoever does not reach that level is a disbeliever, not judged to have faith. For this reason they judged all common Muslims as disbelievers. And they added to this what is even more disgraceful: they denied from the excellences of the Messenger of Allah what was specific to him beyond the prophets, such as the occurrence of the Mi'raj, the affirmation of his intercession on the Day of Resurrection, the existence of the Kawthar Pool; and they denied the reports and traditions that have come concerning these topics; and they also denied the punishment of the grave and the Qur'anic texts concerning that.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage continues Baqillani's indictment of the Mu'tazila by listing three more agreed-upon disgraceful views. First, they assert that many duties (like rational reflection and gratitude) are obligatory without a divine command, implying reason suffices independently of revelation—a rejection of the necessity of revealed law. Second, they make faith conditional upon comprehensive theological knowledge, equating the laity with scholars like Abu Hudhayl and al-Nazzam; anyone falling short is deemed a disbeliever, leading to the takfir (excommunication) of all common Muslims. Third, they deny specific prophetic miracles and eschatological realities: the Night Journey (Mi'raj), intercession, the Kawthar Pool, and the punishment of the grave, despite clear textual proofs. This shows their rationalist excess and disregard for transmitted sources.

Key Terms
وجوب النظر
الاستدلال
شكر المنعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that all moral duties are knowable by reason alone.
Correction: They only claimed some duties are rationally obligatory, not all. However, this still conflicts with the Ash'ari view that all obligations stem from divine command.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila view reported by Baqillani, what makes certain acts obligatory?
A) Divine command alone
B) Reason, even without a divine command
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Prophetic tradition

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claimed rational obligation independent of revelation, which Baqillani condemns as a disgraceful innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a deep inner sense that you should be grateful to God, do you treat that as a mere rational impulse or as a divine command? How would your gratitude change if you saw it as an act of worship demanded by your Creator?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that reason alone could obligate you to seek truth and avoid disbelief. Have you ever followed your own reasoning on a moral or spiritual matter without checking whether Allah has commanded it? What might you be missing when you do that?
?
You likely already believe gratitude to God is important. But is your gratitude a response to a rational sense of fairness, or is it an obedient act of worship seeking His pleasure? How can you transform your next moment of gratitude into a commanded act that draws you closer to Him?

Mu'tazila on Perfect Knowledge Required for Faith
Baqillani explains the Mu'tazila condition for faith and its consequence of takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that faith requires comprehensive theological knowledge, leading to the excommunication of common Muslims, and why this is problematic.

Core Idea
Requiring scholarly knowledge for faith excommunicates most Muslims.

The second disgraceful view Baqillani lists is the Mu'tazila claim that one cannot be a believer unless they possess the same level of theological expertise as their leading scholars. This leads them to declare all lay Muslims disbelievers. In this lesson, we analyze this harsh stance and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the condition for faith: knowing all conditions, reaching the level of scholars like Abu Hudhayl and al-Nazzam, being able to establish proofs and debate.
2
Identify the condition for faith
The Mu'tazila make faith dependent on comprehensive knowledge and argumentative ability. Why might this be unrealistic?
3
Understand the consequence: takfir
Because few can meet this standard, they label the vast majority of Muslims as disbelievers. Reflect on the severity of this.
4
See the contrast with orthodox view
In Sunni Islam, faith is valid with minimal knowledge (e.g., belief in the basics). This Mu'tazila position is extremely exclusivist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم قولهم : إن كثيراً من الأشياء تجب على العبد من غير أن يكون من أمر الله تعالى فيه أمر مثل النظر والاستدلال وشكر المنعم، وترك الكفر والكفران . ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم قولهم : إن العبد لا يحصل له صفة الإيمان حتى يعلم جميع ما هو شرط في اعتقادهم ويبلغ في معرفته درجة علمائهم كأبي هذيل والنظام وغيرهما، ويقدر فيه على تقرير الدلالة ويتمكن من المناظرة ،والمجادلة، ومن لم يبلغ تلك الدرجة كان كافراً لا يحكم له بالإيمان ولهذا حكموا بالكفر على جميع عوام المسلمين ومما زادوا على هذا ما هو أفضح منه فأنكروا من مفاخر رسول الله ما كان مختصاً به زائداً على الأنبيـاء كـوجـود المعراج، وثبوت الشفاعة له يوم القيامة ووجود حوض الكوثر وأنكروا ما ورد في هذه الأبواب الآثار والأخبار وأنكروا عذاب القبر أيضاً وما في ذلك من نصوص قرآنية.
And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed many things are obligatory upon the servant without there being any command from Allah, exalted, concerning them, such as reflection, rational inquiry, gratitude to the Benefactor, and abandoning disbelief and ingratitude. And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed the servant does not attain the quality of faith until he knows all that is a condition in their belief and reaches in his knowledge the level of their scholars like Abu Hudhayl, al-Nazzam, and others, and becomes able to establish the proof and capable of debate and argumentation; and whoever does not reach that level is a disbeliever, not judged to have faith. For this reason they judged all common Muslims as disbelievers. And they added to this what is even more disgraceful: they denied from the excellences of the Messenger of Allah what was specific to him beyond the prophets, such as the occurrence of the Mi'raj, the affirmation of his intercession on the Day of Resurrection, the existence of the Kawthar Pool; and they denied the reports and traditions that have come concerning these topics; and they also denied the punishment of the grave and the Qur'anic texts concerning that.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage continues Baqillani's indictment of the Mu'tazila by listing three more agreed-upon disgraceful views. First, they assert that many duties (like rational reflection and gratitude) are obligatory without a divine command, implying reason suffices independently of revelation—a rejection of the necessity of revealed law. Second, they make faith conditional upon comprehensive theological knowledge, equating the laity with scholars like Abu Hudhayl and al-Nazzam; anyone falling short is deemed a disbeliever, leading to the takfir (excommunication) of all common Muslims. Third, they deny specific prophetic miracles and eschatological realities: the Night Journey (Mi'raj), intercession, the Kawthar Pool, and the punishment of the grave, despite clear textual proofs. This shows their rationalist excess and disregard for transmitted sources.

Key Terms
صفة الإيمان
تكفير العوام
أبو هذيل
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila were lenient on takfir, but here they excommunicate all lay Muslims.
Correction: In fact, they had a very strict definition of faith that excluded most people.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what level of knowledge did the Mu'tazila require for faith?
A) Knowledge of the basics of Islam
B) Knowledge equal to that of their leading scholars
C) Knowledge of only the Quran
D) Knowledge of hadith

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila made faith dependent on advanced theology, leading to the excommunication of common Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself looking down on fellow Muslims who lack deep Islamic knowledge, forgetting that true faith is in the heart? What would it take for you to change that attitude starting today?
?
If the Mu'tazila's standard were applied to you, would you be considered a believer? Does that thought humble you or challenge your reliance on knowledge alone?
?
On the Day of Judgment, will your salvation depend on your ability to argue theology or on the sincerity of your submission to Allah? What step can you take right now to strengthen your heart's connection over your mind's arguments?

Mu'tazila Denial of Prophetic Miracles and Eschatology
Baqillani lists Mu'tazila denials of prophetic miracles and eschatological realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila denial of specific prophetic miracles and eschatological realities such as the Night Journey, intercession, the Kawthar Pool, and the punishment of the grave.

Core Idea
Denying prophetic miracles undermines the honor of the Prophet and revelation.

Baqillani presents a third Mu'tazila position even more disgraceful: they deny events unique to Prophet Muhammad like the Mi'raj and intercession, as well as the punishment of the grave, despite clear Quranic and hadith evidence. This lesson explores these denials and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the list of denied items: Mi'raj, intercession, Kawthar Pool, and punishment of the grave.
2
Understand the nature of the denials

3
Reflect on the significance
These are core beliefs for Sunni Muslims. Denying them challenges the authenticity of revelation and the Prophet's status.
4
Appreciate Baqillani's tone
He calls these views 'even more disgraceful.' This shows the severity of the deviation in his eyes.

Mu'tazila on Eternal Punishment for Grave Sinners
Baqillani reports the Mu'tazila stance on eternal punishment for grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine of eternal punishment for grave sinners, including their reasoning that even monotheistic Muslims can have all their good deeds nullified by one major sin.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila say one major sin can nullify a lifetime of faith and obedience.

Baqillani begins by reporting a severe position held by the Mu'tazila regarding the fate of Muslims who commit major sins. According to them, even a believing monotheist will be eternally punished if he dies unrepentant, with all his good deeds wiped out. This passage explains that extreme view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist of their view: eternal punishment for grave sinners regardless of faith.
Notice the examples of 'minor' sins like drinking wine or withholding zakah.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify that the Mu'tazila include tawheed, belief, and all acts of obedience being invalidated by one sin.
3
Reflect on the severity
Consider the implications: according to them, a single gulp of wine wipes out all rewards. What does that say about God's justice?
This sets up the rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا كلهم بتخليد كل من اقترف كبيرةً ومات مُصرّاً عليها، وإن كان مسلماً موحداً مصدّقاً الله تعالى ولرسوله عليه السلام ولجميع من عنده عالماً بأن ما ركبه حرام محظور؛ فإن الله يبطل بذلك ثواب توحيده وتصديقه وصلواته وسائر طاعاته ولا يثيبه على شيء أمره بفعله ففعله، بل يأخذه بالذنب الواحد الذي هو شرب جرعة من خمر أو منع خمسة دراهم من الزكاة الواجبة في ماله أو غصب عشرة دراهم، على حسب اختلافهم في الوعيد ردّاً لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ خَيْرٌ مِنْهَا﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿أَنِّي لَا أُضِيعُ عَمَلَ عَامِلٍ مِنْكُمْ مِنْ ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنْثَى﴾، في نظائر لهذه الآيات.
And they all said concerning the eternal abiding (takhleed) of everyone who commits a major sin (kabīra) and dies persisting upon it, even if he is a Muslim, a monotheist, believing in God, exalted, and His Messenger, peace be upon him, and all that is with Him, knowing that what he committed is forbidden and prohibited: then God invalidates by that the reward of his tawheed, his affirmation, his prayers, and all his other acts of obedience, and He does not reward him for anything He commanded him to do and he did it; rather, He takes him to task for the single sin, which is drinking a gulp of wine or withholding five dirhams of the obligatory zakah from his wealth or usurping ten dirhams, according to their differences regarding the threat, in rejection of His saying: 'Verily good deeds remove evil deeds' (11:114), and His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have better than it' (27:89), and His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times its like' (6:160), and His saying: 'I do not waste the deed of any doer among you, male or female' (3:195), and similar verses.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage reports the Mu'tazila position on the fate of grave sinners (murtakibū al-kabā'ir). They all agree that anyone who commits a major sin and dies without repentance will be eternally punished in Hell, even if he is a believing Muslim. According to them, God nullifies all his good deeds—including tawheed, belief, prayers, and obedience—and punishes him solely for that one sin, with no reward for his obedience. Baqillani then refutes this by citing four Qur'anic verses that affirm that good deeds remove bad deeds and that God rewards good deeds manifold and does not waste the deeds of believers. The Mu'tazila position is thus shown to contradict explicit Quranic promises of mercy and reward.

Key Terms
تخليد (takhleed)
كبيرة (kabīra)
مصرّ (muṣirr)
وعيد (wa'īd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only the Khawarij held that grave sinners are eternally punished.
Correction: The Mu'tazila also held this view, though they considered the person in a state between faith and disbelief (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn).

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what happens to a Muslim who dies persisting in a major sin?
A) He enters paradise after some punishment
B) All his good deeds are nullified and he is eternally punished
C) He is forgiven automatically
D) He becomes a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila taught that a single unrepented major sin nullifies all good deeds and leads to eternal punishment, even for a believing Muslim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying on your good deeds—your prayer, charity, or faith—as a safety net while knowingly persisting in a major sin? What does the Mu'tazila position force you to confront about that attitude?
?
If you truly believed that one unrepented major sin could erase all your good deeds, how would your approach to sin and repentance change starting today?
?
Consider a sin you might be downplaying. Even if you disagree with the Mu'tazila, their extreme view highlights the gravity of sin. Will you now treat that sin as seriously as they do? What concrete step will you take to address it?

Refuting Mu'tazila: Quranic Verses on Good Deeds
Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazila using four Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine by citing four Qur'anic verses that affirm the removal of sins by good deeds and God's multiplication of rewards.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly states that good deeds erase bad deeds, contradicting the Mu'tazila.

After reporting the Mu'tazila position, Baqillani immediately refutes it by invoking Qur'anic verses. He cites four verses that affirm the power of good deeds to remove sins, the multiplication of reward, and that God does not waste the deeds of believers. This passage demonstrates how scriptural evidence is used to counter rationalist sectarian positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the four verses cited by Baqillani and note the key phrases: 'good deeds remove evil deeds,' 'whoever does good will have better,' 'ten times the like,' and 'I do not waste the deed of any doer.'
These are direct contradictions to the Mu'tazila claim.
2
Understand the refutation
The verses show that God rewards good deeds and does not nullify them; rather, good deeds erase sins. This challenges the idea that a single sin nullifies all good deeds.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Baqillani uses Quranic proofs. This is a common Ash'ari method: cite clear scriptural texts against speculative theology.
The verses are from Surah Hud, Surah An-Naml, Surah Al-An'am, and Surah Aal-e-Imran.

Source Text

The Linguistic and Legal Meaning of Iman
Author gives linguistic and legal definitions of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman (faith) linguistically means affirmation (tasdiq) and legally means affirming the Messenger in what he brought necessarily.

Core Idea
Iman means affirmation — of the heart, not just words.

Before we dive into the debates about what constitutes iman, the author establishes the basic foundation: the word itself has a clear linguistic meaning and a technical legal meaning. This lesson covers both, grounding the discussion in Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author starts with a hypothetical question.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage gives a linguistic definition and a legal definition. Find each one and note the evidence provided.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the author distinguishes between linguistic and legal meanings. This helps clarify that theological terms can have both broad and technical senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإيمان والإسلام والأسماء والأحكام. باب القول في معنى الإيمان. فإن قال قائل : خبّرونا ما الإيمان عندكم؟ اعلم أن الإيمان في اللغة التصديق قال تعالى حكاية عن إخوة يوسف وما أنت بمؤمن لنا أي بمصدق، وقال عليه السلام : الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله أي تصدق، وأما في الشرع فهو التصديق للرسول فيما علم مجيئه به ضرورة، فتفصيلا فيما علم تفصيلاً، وإجمالاً فيما علم إجمالاً. وقيل هو المعرفة. فقوم بالله، وقوم بالله وبما جاءت به الرسل. وقالت الكرامية : هو كلمتا الشهادة. وقالت طائفة : التصديق مع الكلمتين ويروى هذا عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وقال قوم : إنه أعمال الجوارح فذهب الخوارج والعلاف وعبد الجبار إلى أنه الطاعات فرضاً أو نقلاً، وذهب الجبائي وأكثر المعتزلة البصرية إلى أنه الطاعات المفترضة دون النوافل. وقال السلف وأصحاب الأثر إنه مجموع هذه الثلاثة فهو تصديق بالجنان وإقرار باللسان وعمل بالأركان. ووجه الضبط : إن الإيمان عن فعل القلب والجوارح فهو إما فعل القلب فقط وهو المعرفة أو التصديق، وأما فعل الجوارح فقط، وهو إما اللسان وهو الكلمتان أو غيره وهو العمل بالطاعات، وأما فعل القلب والجوارح معاً والجارحة إما اللسان أو سائر الجوارح.
The chapter on speech regarding belief (iman), Islam, names, and rulings. The chapter on the statement concerning the meaning of belief (iman). For if a questioner says: Inform us, what is belief (al-iman) according to you? Know that belief (al-iman) in language is affirmation (al-tasdiq). Allah the Exalted said, narrating from the brothers of Yusuf: 'And you are not a believer (mu'min) to us' meaning a confirmer (musaddiq). And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Belief (iman) is that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, and His messengers' meaning you affirm. As for in the Sacred Law, it is affirmation (al-tasdiq) of the Messenger regarding what is known necessarily to have come from him, in detail for what is known in detail, and in summary for what is known in summary. And it is said that it is knowledge (al-ma'rifa). Some say [it is knowledge] of Allah, and some say of Allah and of what the messengers brought. And the Karramiyya said: It is the two words of witness (kalimata al-shahada). And a group said: Affirmation (al-tasdiq) along with the two words, and this is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And some people said: It is the actions of the limbs; the Khawarij, al-Allaf, and Abd al-Jabbar went to the view that it is acts of obedience, whether obligatory or supererogatory; and al-Jubba'i and most of the Basran Mu'tazila went to the view that it is the obligatory acts of obedience without the supererogatory. And the Salaf and the People of Hadith (ashab al-athar) said: It is the sum of these three: affirmation (tasdiq) by the heart, declaration (iqrar) by the tongue, and action (amal) by the limbs. And the method of control (wajh al-dabt) is that iman concerns the action of the heart and the limbs: it is either an action of the heart alone, which is knowledge (ma'rifa) or affirmation (tasdiq); or an action of the limbs alone, which is either the tongue (the two words) or other than that (acts of obedience); or an action of both heart and limbs together, and the limb is either the tongue or the rest of the limbs.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the meaning of iman (faith). It begins with a hypothetical question: 'What is iman according to you?' The author then gives the linguistic definition: iman means tasdiq (affirmation, assent), supported by a Quranic verse and a hadith. Next, he gives the legal (shar'i) definition: affirmation of the Messenger concerning everything known necessarily to be from him, whether in detail or in summary. Then he notes that some define iman as ma'rifa (knowledge), and within that there is disagreement whether it is knowledge of God alone or also of what the messengers brought. He then lists the positions of various sects: the Karramiyya define it as the two words of the shahada; some (attributed to Abu Hanifa) say tasdiq plus the two words; others say it is actions of the limbs — the Khawarij, al-Allaf, and Abd al-Jabbar hold it includes all acts of obedience (obligatory and supererogatory), while al-Jubba'i and most Basran Mu'tazila restrict it to obligatory acts only. The Salaf and Ahl al-Hadith say it is the combination of three: heart's affirmation, tongue's declaration, and limbs' actions. Finally, the author provides a systematic classification (wajh al-dabt): iman is either an action of the heart alone (ma'rifa or tasdiq), or an action of the limbs alone (tongue's two words, or other acts of obedience), or an action of both heart and limbs together (tongue or other limbs). This classification sets the stage for the arguments that follow.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is simply verbal confession without inner affirmation.
Correction: The author shows that the core meaning is affirmation, which is an inner act.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the linguistic meaning of iman?
A) Knowledge (ma'rifa)
B) Affirmation (tasdiq)
C) The two words of testimony
D) Actions of the limbs

Key Takeaway
Iman linguistically and legally centers on tasdiq (affirmation) of the Messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman is fundamentally inner affirmation, not just words, are you truly affirming the Messenger's message in your heart? What specific aspects of his message do you struggle to affirm deeply?
?
The author distinguishes between detailed and summary affirmation. Have you lazily accepted some beliefs in summary without really affirming them in detail? Which areas of your faith need deeper, detailed conviction?
?
The brothers of Yusuf accused him of not being a 'mu'min' to them. When you hear the Quran, do you feel accused in the same way? Are you genuinely a 'musaddiq' to Allah's words, or just going through the motions?

Sectarian Views on the Definition of Iman
Author enumerates various sectarian definitions of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will have surveyed the major theological positions on what constitutes iman, including those who define it as knowledge, as the two words of testimony, and as actions of the limbs.

Core Idea
Different sects reduced iman to one dimension; the author catalogues them.

After establishing the foundational definition, the author proceeds to list the various opinions held by different groups. This lesson takes you through the views of those who defined iman as ma'rifa (knowledge), as the two words of shahada, as tasdiq plus the two words, and as actions of the limbs (with internal disagreements among the Mu'tazila and Khawarij).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the views
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author lists views one after another.
2
Identify each group and their definition
Go through each view: know that some say it is knowledge (ma'rifa), some say it is the two words, some say it is actions. Note the subgroups.
Pay attention to the names of sects and individuals.
3
Reflect on the reductionism
Consider how each view reduces iman to a single component (heart, tongue, or limbs). The author is setting up for the comprehensive Salaf view.

The chapter on speech regarding belief (iman), Islam, names, and rulings. The chapter on the statement concerning the meaning of belief (iman). For if a questioner says: Inform us, what is belief (al-iman) according to you? Know that belief (al-iman) in language is affirmation (al-tasdiq). Allah the Exalted said, narrating from the brothers of Yusuf: 'And you are not a believer (mu'min) to us' meaning a confirmer (musaddiq). And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Belief (iman) is that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, and His messengers' meaning you affirm. As for in the Sacred Law, it is affirmation (al-tasdiq) of the Messenger regarding what is known necessarily to have come from him, in detail for what is known in detail, and in summary for what is known in summary. And it is said that it is knowledge (al-ma'rifa). Some say [it is knowledge] of Allah, and some say of Allah and of what the messengers brought. And the Karramiyya said: It is the two words of witness (kalimata al-shahada). And a group said: Affirmation (al-tasdiq) along with the two words, and this is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And some people said: It is the actions of the limbs; the Khawarij, al-Allaf, and Abd al-Jabbar went to the view that it is acts of obedience, whether obligatory or supererogatory; and al-Jubba'i and most of the Basran Mu'tazila went to the view that it is the obligatory acts of obedience without the supererogatory. And the Salaf and the People of Hadith (ashab al-athar) said: It is the sum of these three: affirmation (tasdiq) by the heart, declaration (iqrar) by the tongue, and action (amal) by the limbs. And the method of control (wajh al-dabt) is that iman concerns the action of the heart and the limbs: it is either an action of the heart alone, which is knowledge (ma'rifa) or affirmation (tasdiq); or an action of the limbs alone, which is either the tongue (the two words) or other than that (acts of obedience); or an action of both heart and limbs together, and the limb is either the tongue or the rest of the limbs.

The Salaf Position and the Comprehensive Classification of Iman
Author presents Salaf definition and a systematic classification of iman's components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf and Ahl al-Hadith definition of iman as comprising heart, tongue, and limbs, and the systematic classification (wajh al-dabt) that organizes all possible components.

Core Idea
Iman is not one thing but three: heart's affirmation, tongue's declaration, and limbs' action.

The author now presents the view of the Salaf (the early pious predecessors) and the People of Hadith, which synthesizes all previous views. Then he provides a comprehensive classification that shows how every definition fits into categories of heart actions, limb actions, or both. This lesson unpacks that synthesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Salaf definition
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three components: heart, tongue, limbs.
2
Understand the three components
The Salaf view says iman is tasdiq bil-jinan (heart affirmation), iqrar bil-lisan (tongue declaration), and amal bil-arkan (limb actions). These three are inseparable.
3
Study the classification (wajh al-dabt)
The author then provides a systematic classification: iman is either heart action alone, limb action alone, or both. Each category includes specific components mentioned earlier.
This classification encompasses all the earlier sectarian views.
4
Reflect on the synthesis
Consider how the Salaf view integrates all previous views into a comprehensive whole. This is the author's preferred position.

Three Quranic Proofs That Iman Is in the Heart
Imam al-Baqillani cites three Quranic proofs that iman is located in the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three Quranic proofs that iman (faith) is fundamentally a matter of the heart, distinct from external actions.

Core Idea
Faith dwells in the heart, not in deeds alone.

Imam al-Baqillani presents three clear arguments from the Quran to settle a foundational question: is iman merely outward action, or is it something deeper? The answer, drawn directly from the Book of Allah, is that iman resides in the heart and is distinct from righteous deeds. Let's examine each proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that each proof begins with a specific sign from the Quran.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage presents three arguments (wujuh). Find each one and notice the Quranic evidence used: verses about the heart, coupling with actions, and conjoining with necessity.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why these three proofs are important. They establish that iman is not merely outward action but a distinct reality in the heart. The commentary will help you see the logic.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا وجوه الأول: الايات الدالة على محلية القلب للإيمان نحو أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان ، ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبهم وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان ، ومنه الايات الدالة على الختم والطبع على القلوب ويؤيده دعاء النبي : اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك وقوله لاسامه ، وقد قتل من قال لا إله إلا الله هلا شققت على قلبه . والثاني : جاء الإيمان مقروناً بالعمل الصالح في غير موضع من الكتاب نحو الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات فدل على التغاير. والثالث : أنه قرن بضر العمل الصالح نحو وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا ومنه مفهوم قوله : الذين آمنوا ولم يلبسوا إيمانهم بظلم .
We have arguments: First, the verses indicating that the heart is the locus of faith (iman), such as: 'Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed faith' and 'When faith enters their hearts' and 'His heart is at peace with faith', and from these are the verses indicating the sealing and stamping upon hearts. This is supported by the Prophet's supplication: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' and his statement to Usama, when he killed someone who said 'There is no god but Allah': 'Did you split open his heart?' Second, faith (iman) is mentioned coupled with righteous action in many places in the Book, such as 'those who believe and do righteous deeds', which indicates a distinction (taghayur). Third, it is conjoined with the necessity of righteous action, such as 'And if two parties of the believers fight' and from it the implication of His statement: 'those who believe and do not mix their faith with injustice.'
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic proofs (wujuh) that iman is fundamentally an affair of the heart, distinct from actions. The first proof uses verses that explicitly connect iman with the heart: the heart is where iman is written, entered, and where it settles. The Prophet's supplication to 'make my heart firm' and his rebuke to Usama for not splitting open the heart to check for faith further support this. The second proof notes that iman and righteous actions are often mentioned together as distinct things (e.g., 'those who believe and do righteous deeds'), showing they are not identical. The third proof shows that iman is conjoined with the necessity of righteous action, yet still distinguished; fighting believers are still called believers, and the condition of not mixing faith with injustice implies faith can exist even with imperfection. These three proofs together establish that iman is a separate reality in the heart, not reducible to actions.

Key Terms
محلية القلب
الإيمان
القلب
العمل الصالح
التغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman is nothing but outward actions or speech.
Correction: The Quran repeatedly links iman to the heart and distinguishes it from actions, as shown by these three proofs.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof (al-ثاني) establish by coupling iman with righteous actions?
A) That iman is the same as actions
B) That iman and actions are distinct (taghayur)
C) That iman is only verbal
D) That actions are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Iman is a reality in the heart, not reducible to actions, as shown by three distinct Quranic proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith based on their external actions? How does the Prophet's rebuke to Usama—'Did you split open his heart?'—challenge your tendency to assess others' iman? Will you repent from this judgment and leave hidden faith to Allah alone?
?
Your heart may appear firm in public, but in private, are you confident that iman is inscribed within you? If your actions were to falter, would you still hold onto the belief that your iman resides in your heart, or would you despair? Ask Allah tonight to make your heart firm, as the Prophet did.
?
The second proof shows that iman and righteous deeds are distinct yet coupled. In your life, do you treat righteous deeds as a separate fruit of iman or do you rely on them as if they are iman itself? How can you ensure your deeds are an expression of the faith in your heart, not a substitute for it?

The Necessity of Inner Affirmation (Tasdiq Qalbi)
An objection claiming iman is only verbal affirmation and the author's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that true tasdiq (affirmation) cannot be merely verbal and must include inner conviction, supported by logical reasoning and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
True iman requires heartfelt affirmation, not just words.

This lesson presents an objection from language: if the word tasdiq only means verbal utterance, why isn't iman just that? The author refutes this by showing that the very concept of affirmation logically necessitates inner belief, and the Quran confirms that verbal claims alone can be false.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the opening objection carefully: why would someone argue that iman is only verbal affirmation based on language?
The objector appeals to the common understanding of 'tasdiq' in Arabic as verbal utterance.
2
Follow the Logical Counterargument
The author uses a hypothetical: even if the word 'sadaqtu' had no meaning or a different meaning, uttering it would not make one a muṣaddiq. So tasdiq must be more than just the word.
Notice the logical necessity: either tasdiq is the meaning of the word, or the word indicates its meaning.
3
Recognize the Rational Necessity
From this, rational people must affirm inner tasdiq (al-tasdiq al-qalbi) as essential. The author states this as a certainty.
This step moves from linguistic analysis to a universal rational principle.
4
Examine the Quranic Evidence
The author cites verses about hypocrites and Bedouins who verbally claimed faith but were not true believers, proving inner affirmation is necessary.
These verses directly refute the idea that mere words suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فلم لا تجعلونه التصديق بالله فإن أهل اللغة لا يعلمون من التصديق إلا ذلك؟ قلنا لو فرض عدم وضع صدقت لمعنى أو وضعه لمعنى غير التصديق لم يكن المتلفظ به مصدقاً قطعاً، فالتصديق إما معنى هذه اللفظة أو هذه اللفظة لدلالتها على معناه، فيجب الجزم بعلم العقلاء ضرورة التصديق القلبي ويؤيده قوله تعالى ومن الناس من يقول آمنا بالله واليوم الآخر وما هم بمؤمنين ) وقوله : قالت الاعراب آمنا ) الآية .
If it is said: Why do you not consider it [iman] to be affirmation of Allah? For the people of language know nothing of affirmation (tasdiq) except that? We say: If it were hypothesized that 'sadaqtu' was not coined for a meaning, or was coined for a meaning other than affirmation, then the one who utters it would certainly not be a confirmer (musaddiq). So affirmation (tasdiq) is either the meaning of this word or this word indicates its meaning. Therefore, one must be certain, by the knowledge of rational people, of the necessity of inner affirmation (al-tasdiq al-qalbi). This is supported by His statement: 'And among people are those who say we believe in Allah and the Last Day, but they are not believers' and His statement: 'The bedouins say we believe' — the verse.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the author's response. The objector argues: Why don't you (the author) define iman as verbal affirmation of God, since the Arabic language knows no other meaning of tasdiq? The author answers with a logical argument: even if the word 'sadaqtu' (I affirmed) were not coined for any meaning, or were coined for a meaning other than tasdiq, the person uttering it would not be a mu saddiq (affirmer) at all. So the reality of tasdiq is either the meaning conveyed by the word or the word itself indicating its meaning. In either case, rational people must affirm that inner, heartfelt tasdiq is necessary. The author then supports this with Quranic verses showing that people can verbally claim belief while not being true believers (the hypocrites and the Bedouins), proving that mere verbal utterance is insufficient.

أهل اللغة
التصديق القلبي
المتلفظ
العقلاء

Misconception: Iman is merely verbal affirmation of God.
Correction: The author argues that rational necessity and Quranic evidence show that true iman requires inner heartfelt affirmation; verbal utterance alone is insufficient.

Refuting the Karramiyya: Outward Words vs. Inner Faith
The Karramiyya object that uttering the two words suffices for faith; the author refutes with consensus, Quran, and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya argument that mere utterance of the two words suffices for faith, and the author's refutation showing that outward acceptance does not determine inner reality.

Core Idea
Mere utterance does not guarantee true faith; the heart matters.

The Karramiyya were a sect that insisted that simply saying the two shahada words makes one a believer, regardless of inner state. In this passage, Imam Baqillani refutes this position using consensus, the Quran, and logical reasoning. This lesson unpacks their objection and his powerful response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Karramiyya argument
Read the first sentence where they argue that the Prophet and early generations accepted the two words without question. Note the word 'tawatur' (mass transmission).
This is their core claim.
2
Examine the author's first counter
The author counters with the consensus (ijma) that the hypocrite (munafiq) is a disbeliever, even though they uttered the two words. This shows outward acceptance does not equal true faith.
The munafiq openly says the shahada but is inwardly kafir.
3
Consider the Quranic evidence
The author cites the verse 'Say: You have not believed, but rather say I submit' (49:14). This shows a distinction between verbal submission and actual faith.
The Qur'an itself distinguishes.
4
Analyze the logical consequence
The author points out that if mere utterance sufficed, then a person who inwardly affirms but is prevented from speaking (e.g., mute) would be considered a disbeliever—contrary to consensus.
This reductio ad absurdum refutes their claim.
5
Distinguish outward vs. inward reality
The author clarifies that the two words are called 'iman' linguistically and carry outward rulings, but the real dispute is about the state between the person and Allah.
This resolves the ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الكرامية : بأنه تواتر أن الرسول والصحابة والتابعين كانوا يقنعون بالكلمتين ممن أتى بهما لا يستفسرون عن علمه وعمله، فيحكمون بإيمانه بمجرد الكلمتين . الجواب : معارضته بالإجماع على أن المنافق كافر . وبنحو قوله : قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا ) ولا نزاع في أنه يسمى إيماناً لغة، وأنه يترتب عليه أحكام الإيمان ظاهراً، وإنما النزاع فيما بينه وبين الله ثم نقول: يلزمكم أن من صدق بقلبه وهم بالتكلم بالكلمتين فمنعه مانع من خرس وغيره أن يكون كافراً، وهو خلاف الإجماع .
The Karramiyya argued: It is established by tawatur (mass transmission) that the Messenger, the Companions, and the Followers were satisfied with the two words [of witness] from whoever uttered them, without inquiring about his knowledge or action, and they judged him a believer by the mere two words. The answer: This is countered by the consensus that the hypocrite (munafiq) is a disbeliever (kafir). And by His statement: 'Say: You have not believed, but rather say I submit (aslamna).' And there is no dispute that it is called iman linguistically, and that the outward rulings of iman are applied to it; rather the dispute concerns [the reality] between him and Allah. Then we say: It would follow from your position that someone who affirms in his heart and intends to utter the two words but is prevented by a hindrance like muteness would be a disbeliever — and this is contrary to consensus.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the Karramiyya argument and its refutation. The Karramiyya claim that the Prophet and early generations accepted mere utterance of the two shahada words as sufficient for calling someone a believer in this world, without checking inner knowledge or actions. The author responds by pointing out the consensus that the munafiq (hypocrite) is considered a kafir despite uttering the words — so outward acceptance does not imply inner reality. He also cites the Quranic verse that distinguishes between saying 'We believe' and being true believers. The author then states there is no dispute that linguistically the two words can be called iman, and outwardly the rulings of iman apply, but the real dispute is about the inner state. Finally, he points out a logical consequence: if the two words were sufficient, then a person who inwardly affirms but is prevented from speaking (e.g., mute) would be considered a disbeliever, which contradicts consensus. Thus the Karramiyya position is refuted.

الإجماع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the Prophet outwardly accepted the shahada from people, inner faith is not required for being a believer.
Correction: The author shows that outward acceptance does not determine inner reality; consensus on hypocrites and the Quranic verse prove that verbal submission can be separate from true faith.

Checkpoint

B) The mute would be a disbeliever under their view, which is against consensus
C) The mute must perform actions
D) The mute is excused from iman

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya position that mere utterance suffices for faith is refuted by consensus on hypocrites, Quranic distinction, and the absurd consequence that a mute sincere person would be a disbeliever.

Resolving Increase and Decrease of Iman
The author resolves the debate on iman's increase/decrease by distinguishing tasdiq from actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate on iman's increase and decrease is resolved by distinguishing between tasdiq (core affirmation) and the state resulting from actions.

Core Idea
Iman's core affirmation does not vary; variation lies in the state from actions.

The question of whether faith increases and decreases has long been debated. This passage, citing Imam al-Razi, offers a resolution that hinges on how we define iman. By distinguishing between the minimal reality of tasdiq and the mature disposition from actions, the author shows both sides can be valid in their own sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the shift from quoting Imam Razi to stating the truth.
2
Identify the two definitions considered
The author presents two definitions of iman: tasdiq (affirmation) and actions. For each, determine whether increase/decrease is possible.
Imam Razi's statement shows the logical consequence of each definition.
3
Understand the resolution
The author's truth: tasdiq is a single reality with no variation. Variation occurs only in the state (hal) from actions, not in the core affirmation.
The key term 'hal' refers to the condition of the heart that can strengthen.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. It preserves the unity of iman while allowing for growth in faith quality.
This resolves the apparent contradiction between different scholarly positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هل الإيمان يزيد وينقص؟ أثبته طائفة ونفاه آخرون. قال الإمام الرازي وكثير من المتكلمين : هو فرع تفسير الإيمان، فإن قلنا هو التصديق فلا يقبلهما لأن الواجب هو التعيين وأنه لا يقبل التفاوت لأن التفاوت إنما هو لاحتمال النقيض، وهو ولو بأبعد وجه ينافي اليقين. وإن قلنا هو الأعمال فيقبلهما وهو ظاهر . فالحق أن الإيمان الذي هو التصديق فلا تفاوت فيه الذي هو أول مراتبه فمن اعتبر أوائل الأسماء وحمله على التصديق منع من التفاوت كما قال أئمة المتكلمين ومن اعتبر أواخر الأسماء وحمله على الملكة الحاصلة عن الأعمال بحيث ترسخ فيحصل الاتصاف والتحقيق بالإيمان الكامل ظهر له التفاوت وليس ذلك بقادح في اتحاد حقيقته الأولى التي هي التصديق إذ التصديق موجود في جميع رتبه لأنه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم الإيمان وهو المخلص من عهده الكفر والفيصل بين الكافر والمسلم فلا يجزي أقل منه وهو في نفسه حقيقة واحدة لا تتفاوت وإنما التفاوت في الحال الحاصلة عن الأعمال كما قلناه فافهم .
Then, does iman increase and decrease? One group affirmed it and others denied it. Imam al-Razi and many of the mutakallimun said: It is a branch of the interpretation of iman. For if we say it is affirmation (tasdiq), then it does not admit them [increase and decrease] because the obligation is definite (al-ta'yin) and it does not admit variation (tafawut). For variation only occurs due to possibility of the opposite, and that, even in the most remote sense, conflicts with certainty (yaqin). And if we say it is actions, then it admits them, and that is apparent. The truth is that iman which is tasdiq has no variation; it is the first of its stages. So whoever considers the beginnings of the names and takes it as tasdiq prevents variation, as the imams of the mutakallimun said. And whoever considers the later names and takes it as the disposition (malaka) resulting from actions, such that it becomes firmly rooted, and then the attribution and realization of complete iman occurs, to him variation appears. But that does not detract from the unity of its first reality which is tasdiq; tasdiq is present in all its ranks because it is the least thing to which the name iman is applied, and it is what saves from the covenant of disbelief and is the decisive factor between the disbeliever and the Muslim. Nothing less than it suffices, and it is in itself a single reality that does not vary; variation only occurs in the state (hal) resulting from actions, as we have said. So understand.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage resolves the debate on whether iman increases and decreases. Imam al-Razi and many mutakallimun say the answer depends on how iman is defined. If iman is defined as tasdiq (heartfelt affirmation), increase/decrease are impossible because tasdiq admits no gradation — it is either present or absent, and any possibility of the opposite would undermine certainty (yaqin). If iman is defined as actions, then it obviously admits increase and decrease. The author then gives the truth (al-haqq): iman as tasdiq has no variation; it is the minimal reality that qualifies a person as a believer, the 'first stage'. Some scholars consider the early names (or early meanings) and take iman as tasdiq, so they deny variation. Others consider the later names (the mature state of faith) and take iman as the firm disposition (malaka) resulting from actions, so they affirm variation. Both are valid in their own contexts, and this does not compromise the unity of the core reality, which is tasdiq. Tasdiq remains single and non-variable across all ranks; the variation is only in the state (hal) produced by actions. The author concludes with 'fahham' (so understand).

Key Terms
الزيادة
النقصان
التصديق
التفاوت
اليقين
الملكة
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the debate on iman's increase/decrease is purely binary with no possible reconciliation.
Correction: The author shows that both views can be reconciled by distinguishing between the core affirmation (tasdiq), which does not vary, and the state from actions, which does vary.

Checkpoint
According to the resolution, what does not vary in iman?
A) The actions
B) The disposition (malaka)
C) The affirmation (tasdiq)
D) The state (hal)

Key Takeaway
Iman as tasdiq is a single, non-varying reality; any variation is in the experiential state from actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have tasdiq in your heart, but do you neglect the actions that strengthen your iman's state? If you die with only minimal tasdiq, are you content? What change will you make today to move from minimal to complete iman?

When you feel your iman is weak, do you doubt the presence of your tasdiq? How does this lesson reassure you that your core affirmation remains intact, yet challenge you to improve your actions?
?
Is your tasdiq so firm that no possibility of its opposite exists, or do you entertain doubts? If there is any remote possibility of disbelief in your heart, then your tasdiq may not be genuine. What are you going to do to eliminate that possibility?

The Mute Objection: Is Utterance Necessary for Iman?
Baqillani presents a reductio against requiring utterance for iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reductio against requiring verbal utterance for iman and its implication for the definition of iman.

Core Idea
A mute who believes in heart cannot be a disbeliever.

Imam Baqillani raises a powerful objection against those who hold that iman requires verbal utterance. He argues that if a person believes in his heart and intends to speak the shahada but is prevented by muteness, the opposing view would entail declaring him a disbeliever — which contradicts the consensus of the scholars. This lesson examines this objection and its implications for the definition of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The passage is short but precise.
Focus on the logical structure: if X then Y, but Y is false by consensus.
2
Identify the reductio
Baqillani shows that requiring utterance leads to an absurd conclusion: a mute believer would be considered a disbeliever. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect to the definition of iman
This objection suggests that iman cannot require verbal utterance; inner assent (tasdiq) is sufficient for the reality of iman.
This sets the stage for the discussion of increase/decrease that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: يلزمكم أن من صدق بقلبه وهم بالتكلم بالكلمتين فمنعه مانع من خرس وغيره أن يكون كافراً، وهو خلاف الإجماع . ثم هل الإيمان يزيد وينقص؟ أثبته طائفة ونفاه آخرون. قال الإمام الرازي وكثير من المتكلمين : هو فرع تفسير الإيمان، فإن قلنا هو التصديق فلا يقبلهما لأن الواجب هو التعيين وأنه لا يقبل التفاوت لأن التفاوت إنما هو لاحتمال النقيض، وهو ولو بأبعد وجه ينافي اليقين. وإن قلنا هو الأعمال فيقبلهما وهو ظاهر . فالحق أن الإيمان الذي هو التصديق فلا تفاوت فيه الذي هو أول مراتبه فمن اعتبر أوائل الأسماء وحمله على التصديق منع من التفاوت كما قال أئمة المتكلمين ومن اعتبر أواخر الأسماء وحمله على الملكة الحاصلة عن الأعمال بحيث ترسخ فيحصل الاتصاف والتحقيق بالإيمان الكامل ظهر له التفاوت وليس ذلك بقادح في اتحاد حقيقته الأولى التي هي التصديق إذ التصديق موجود في جميع رتبه لأنه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم الإيمان وهو المخلص من عهده الكفر والفيصل بين الكافر والمسلم فلا يجزي أقل منه وهو في نفسه حقيقة واحدة لا تتفاوت وإنما التفاوت في الحال الحاصلة عن الأعمال كما قلناه فافهم.
Then we say: It would be necessitated by you that one who assents in his heart and intends to utter the two words but is prevented by a hindrance like muteness or other would be a disbeliever, and that is contrary to consensus. Then: Does iman increase and decrease? A group affirms it and others deny it. Imam al-Razi and many theologians said: It is a branch of the interpretation of iman. If we say it is tasdiq, then it does not admit them (increase and decrease) because the obligation is specification and it does not admit variation, because variation is only due to possibility of contradiction, and that, even in the most remote way, contradicts certainty. And if we say it is actions, then it admits them, and that is apparent. So the truth is that iman which is tasdiq has no variation in it, which is its first rank. So whoever considers the beginning of the name and interprets it as tasdiq prevents variation, as the leading theologians said. And whoever considers the end of the name and interprets it as the disposition (malaka) resulting from actions such that it becomes firmly rooted, then the description and realization of complete iman occurs, variation appears for him. And that is not detrimental to the unity of its first reality which is tasdiq, since tasdiq exists in all its ranks because it is the least of what the name iman is applied to, and it is what saves from the covenant of disbelief and the criterion between the disbeliever and the Muslim. So nothing less than it suffices, and it is in itself a single reality that does not vary. Variation is only in the state resulting from actions, as we said. So understand.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a discussion. First, Baqillani raises a reductio against those who hold that iman is only tasdiq without utterance: if a person believes in his heart and intends to speak the shahada but is prevented by muteness, they would have to consider him a disbeliever, which contradicts consensus. This suggests that something more than inner tasdiq may be needed. Then he transitions to the question of increase and decrease of iman. He reports that some affirm it and others deny it. He cites Imam al-Razi's view that the issue depends on the definition of iman. If iman is tasdiq, it does not admit increase or decrease because tasdiq is a single act of assent that cannot vary; variation would imply doubt. If iman is actions, then it does admit increase and decrease. The correct view is that iman as tasdiq is invariant—it is the minimum that qualifies one as a believer and distinguishes from disbelief. This core is one reality. However, the 'state' (hal) resulting from actions can vary, and people can have more or less complete iman in terms of the disposition (malaka) that comes from works. So there is no contradiction: the essence (tasdiq) is fixed, but its perfection through actions admits degrees. He concludes with 'faham' (understand).

Key Terms
تصديق (tasdiq)
خرس (muteness)
إجماع (consensus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that verbal utterance is a necessary condition for iman, such that without it a person is not a believer.
Correction: The mute objection shows that this is false; a mute who believes in heart and intends to speak is considered a believer by consensus.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's objection, what would the opposing view necessarily conclude about a mute person who believes in his heart and intends to say the shahada?
A) He is a believer because he intended.
B) He is a disbeliever because he did not utter.
C) His status depends on his actions.
D) He is both a believer and a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Inner tasdiq alone is sufficient for the reality of iman; requiring utterance leads to absurdity and contradicts consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own iman, do you secretly measure it by outward actions or words? In light of this lesson, what does that reveal about your understanding of faith? Will you commit to examining your heart's conviction instead of merely your performance?
?
Consider a person you know who is unable to practice Islam outwardly due to a genuine constraint (illness, fear, etc.). Do you ever doubt the reality of their iman? How does the consensus that a mute believer is indeed a believer challenge your internal judgments? What change of heart is required of you?
?
If iman is truly settled in the heart, why do you often feel anxious about your faith when you miss outward acts? Does this anxiety stem from a misplaced requirement? What step will you take today to strengthen your tasdiq, regardless of your ability to express it outwardly?
