الكفور) قلنا: متروك الظاهر إذ يجازي غير الكفور وهو المثاب ولقوله تعالى: (اليوم تجزى كل نفس بما كسبت) الثالث: قوله تعالى بعد إيجاب الحج: (ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين) قلنا المراد من جحد وجوبه. الرابع: (إن العذاب على من كذب وتولى) قلنا: متروك الظاهر للاتفاق على عذاب شارب الخمر والزاني مع أنه غير مكذب الله تعالى: بل لليهود والنصارى. الخامس: قوله تعالى: (فأنذرتكم ناراً تلظى لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى) والفاسق يصلاها قلنا أي: الذي كذب نبي الله محمداً؛ وتولى أي أعرض عن الإيمان؛ وروى الضحاك عن ابن عباس قال: (لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى) أمية بن خلف ونفر من الذين كذبوا محمداً. وقال قتادة: كذب بكتاب الله، وتولى عن طاعة الله. وعن مسلم بن الحسن يقول: سمعت أبا إسحاق الزجاج يقول: هذه الآية التي من أجلها قال أهل الإرجاء بالارجاء، فزعموا أنه لا يدخل النار إلا كافر؛ لقوله جل ثناؤه: (ولا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى) وليس الأمر كما ظنوا هذه نار موصوفة بعينها لا يصلى هذه النار إلا الذي كذب وتولى ولأهل النار منازل؛ فمنها أن المنافقين في الدرك الأسفل من النار، والله سبحانه كل ما وعد عليه بجنس من العذاب فجائز أن يقدر به وقال جل ثناؤه: (وإن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء) فلو كان كل من لم يشرك لم يعذب، لم يكن في قوله: (ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء) فائدة بل كان كلاماً لا معنى له. السادس: وقوله تعالى في حق من خفت موازينه: (ألم تكن آياتي تتلى عليكم فكنتم بها تكذبون) والفاسق ممن خفت موازينه قلنا: بل ثقلت بالإيمان. السابع: وقوله تعالى: (يوم تبيض وجوه وتسود وجوه) والفاسق ممن وجهه مسود. قلنا لا نسلم أن كل فاسق كذلك بل هي واردة في بعض الكفار لقوله: (أكفرتم بعد إيمانكم) الثامن: أنه من أصحاب المشأمة وقال تعالى: (والذين كفروا بآياتنا هم أصحاب المشأمة) وينتقض بالزاني والسارق مع عدم تكذيبهما. التاسع: (ومن كفر بعد ذلك فأولئك الفاسقون) وأنه يقتضي حصر المبتدأ في الخبر قلنا: ممنوع لأن الكافر ابتداء كذلك. معناه أن الكافر الأصلي ابتداءاً هو فاسق حيث إنه ارتكب أكبر الكبائر وهو الكفر فلا وجه لحصر المعنى في فاعل الكبيرة عندهم. العاشر: (إنه لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون) والفاسق آيس من روح الله قلنا: ممنوع للرجاء. الحادي عشر: (إنك من تدخل النار فقد أخزيته) مع قوله: (إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين) قلنا: المفرد المحلى باللام لا عموم له، أو المراد به الخزي الكامل. واحتجت المعتزلة: أن الفاسق ليس مؤمناً لما مر ولا كافراً بالإجماع لأنهم كانوا يقيمون عليه الحد ولا يقتلونه ولا يحكمون بردته ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين قلنا: هو مؤمن وتقدير الكلام فيه في الرد على الخوارج.
Chapter on the statement concerning naming the sinful Muslim a believer. If a questioner says: Inform me about the sinful Muslim, do you call him a believer on account of his faith? [We say:] With us, the committer of a major sin among the people of prayer is a believer, due to His statement the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48). So the disbeliever is not forgiven his sin if he dies upon it, due to His statement the Exalted: 'Indeed, those who disbelieved and averted [people] from the path of Allah and then died while they were disbelievers – never will Allah forgive them' (47:34). And the Messenger says: 'My intercession is for the people of major sins among my ummah.' So this hadith is sufficient for us as evidence that the committer of a major sin is a believer, because intercession is forbidden for the disbelievers. And the Khawarij held that he is a disbeliever, and the Mu'tazila held that he is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. The Khawarij's argument has aspects: First: His statement the Exalted: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers' (5:44). We say: This was revealed entirely concerning the disbelievers; this is established in Sahih Muslim from the hadith of al-Bara'. And upon this is the majority. As for the Muslim, he does not become a disbeliever even if he commits a major sin. And it was said there is an implied [meaning], i.e., 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed' as a rejection of the Qur'an and a denial of the statement of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, he is a disbeliever – said by Ibn Abbas and Mujahid. So the verse is general upon this. Ibn Mas'ud and al-Hasan said: It is general concerning everyone who does not judge by what Allah has revealed among the Muslims, Jews, and disbelievers, i.e., believing that and considering it lawful. As for the one who does that while believing it is a prohibited act, he is among the sinful Muslims, and his affair is to Allah the Exalted; if He wills, He punishes him, and if He wills, He forgives him. And al-Sha'bi said it is specifically concerning the Jews, and al-Nahhas chose this. He said: Three things indicate that: among them that the Jews were mentioned before this in His statement: 'For those who are Jews' (5:44), so the pronoun refers back to them; among them that the context of speech indicates that; except that after it is 'And We prescribed for them' (5:45), so this pronoun is for the Jews by consensus. And also, the Jews are the ones who denied stoning and retaliation. If a questioner says: 'Man' when it is for condition is general unless evidence comes for its specification? It is said to him: 'Man' here is in the sense of 'alladhi' along with what we mentioned of evidence and the implied meaning: 'And the Jews who did not judge by what Allah revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers.' This is among the best of what has been said regarding this. And it is narrated that Hudhayfah was asked about these verses: Are they concerning the Children of Israel? He said: Yes, they are concerning them. And Tawus, Ibn Abbas, and others said: It is not a disbelief that removes from the religion, but it is a disbelief less than disbelief. Second: 'And is recompensed except the ungrateful?' (34:17?) We said: The apparent meaning is abandoned, since non-ungrateful ones are recompensed – they are the rewarded ones – and due to His statement the Exalted: 'Today every soul is recompensed for what it earned' (40:17). Third: His statement the Exalted after the obligation of Hajj: 'And whoever disbelieves – then indeed, Allah is free from need of the worlds' (3:97). We said: The intent is the one who denies its obligation. Fourth: 'Indeed, the punishment is upon those who deny and turn away' (unknown). We said: The apparent meaning is abandoned due to the consensus on the punishment of the wine drinker and the adulterer while they do not deny Allah the Exalted; rather it is for the Jews and Christians. Fifth: His statement the Exalted: 'Then I warned you of a blazing fire. None will burn therein except the most wretched one who denies and turns away' (92:14-16). And the sinful one burns in it. We said: i.e., the one who denied the Prophet of Allah Muhammad and turned away, i.e., turned away from faith. And al-Dahhak narrated from Ibn Abbas who said: 'None will burn therein except the most wretched one' – Umayyah ibn Khalaf and a group of those who denied Muhammad. And Qatada said: He denied the Book of Allah and turned away from obedience to Allah. And from Muslim ibn al-Hasan who says: I heard Abu Ishaq al-Zajjaj say: This is the verse because of which the people of irja' held irja', so they claimed that none enters the Fire except a disbeliever, due to His statement the Exalted: 'and none will burn therein except the most wretched one who denies and turns away' – but the matter is not as they thought. This is a specific described fire; none burns in this fire except the one who denied and turned away. And for the people of the Fire are stations; among them is that the hypocrites are in the lowest depths of the Fire. And Allah the Exalted, everything He promised a type of punishment, it is possible that He decrees it. And He said the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48). So if everyone who did not associate [partners with Allah] were not punished, there would be no benefit in His statement 'and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills'; rather it would be speech without meaning. Sixth: And His statement the Exalted concerning the one whose scales are light: 'Were not My verses recited to you, and you used to deny them?' (23:105) – and the sinful one is among those whose scales are light. We said: Rather they are heavy with faith. Seventh: And His statement the Exalted: 'On the day when faces become white and faces become black' (3:106) – and the sinful one is among those whose face is black. We said: We do not concede that every sinful one is like that; rather it occurs concerning some of the disbelievers, due to His statement: 'Have you disbelieved after your faith?' (3:106). Eighth: That he is among the companions of the left hand, and He said the Exalted: 'And those who disbelieve in Our verses – they are the companions of the left hand' (90:19). And it is contradicted by the adulterer and thief while they do not deny. Ninth: 'And whoever disbelieves after that – then those are the defiantly disobedient' (24:55) – and that it necessitates restricting the subject to the predicate. We said: It is prevented, because the original disbeliever is likewise. Its meaning is that the original disbeliever from the beginning is a fasiq since he committed the greatest of the major sins, which is disbelief. So there is no justification for restricting the meaning to the committer of a major sin according to them. Tenth: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the mercy of Allah except the disbelieving people' (12:87) – and the sinful one despairs of the mercy of Allah. We said: prevented because of hope. Eleventh: 'Indeed, whoever You cause to enter the Fire – You have disgraced him' (3:192) along with His statement: 'Indeed, disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' (16:27). We said: The definite noun with 'al' does not indicate generality, or the intent is complete disgrace. And the Mu'tazila argued: that the sinful one is not a believer for what has passed, nor a disbeliever by consensus, because they would enforce the prescribed punishment on him, not kill him, not judge his apostasy, and bury him in the graveyards of the Muslims. We said: He is a believer, and the estimation of the speech therein is in refutation of the Khawarij.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and a refutation. Imam al-Baqillani first states the Sunni position: the committer of a major sin (fasiq) among the people of prayer is still a believer (mu'min). He cites three proof texts: Quran 4:48 (God forgives less than shirk for whom He wills), Quran 47:34 (disbelievers are not forgiven), and the hadith that the Prophet's intercession is for the people of major sins. Since intercession is forbidden for disbelievers, the hadith implies that the fasiq is a believer. He then notes the Khawarij held the fasiq is a disbeliever, and the Mu'tazila held an intermediate position (neither believer nor disbeliever). The bulk of the passage is a detailed refutation of eleven scriptural arguments used by the Khawarij. For each, al-Baqillani provides a counter-interpretation or indicates that the verse is specific to disbelievers, not applicable to sinful Muslims. He also refutes the Mu'tazila argument that the fasiq is not a believer because he is treated as a Muslim in legal matters (hudud, burial). Al-Baqillani insists the fasiq is a believer, and the legal treatment is consistent with that status; the Mu'tazila's argument actually refutes the Khawarij, not the Sunni position. The overall method is to defend the Sunni creed that faith is not nullified by major sins, relying on the principle that the Qur'anic threats of punishment for such acts do not negate the fundamental belief of the sinner.

Key Terms
خوارج
معتزلة
كفر دون كفر
شفاعة

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani respond to the Mu'tazila argument that the fasiq is not a believer because he is treated as a Muslim in legal matters?
A) He agrees and says the fasiq is not a believer.
B) He says the legal treatment shows he is a believer, and the argument refutes the Khawarij.
C) He says the legal treatment is irrelevant.
D) He says the fasiq is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
All verses used by the Khawarij either refer to disbelievers or are interpreted in a way that does not deny the faith of a sinful Muslim; the Mu'tazila's argument also fails to prove the fasiq is not a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe, but when you sin, do you ever feel that your faith is 'light' or that you are no longer a believer? How does Baqillani's statement that the scales are heavy with faith challenge that feeling? What would it take for you to truly internalize that your faith is not nullified by your sins?
?
The Khawarij cited verses to condemn the sinful Muslim. Have you ever been quick to judge someone's faith based on their sins? How does this lesson force you to reconsider that attitude? Will you now extend hope to others as you hope for yourself?
?
Consider the Mu'tazila argument: they treated the fasiq as neither believer nor disbeliever. Have you ever held a similar lukewarm view of your own faith when you fall into sin? What does Baqillani's insistence that the fasiq is a believer mean for your own hope in Allah's mercy? How can you actively maintain that hope when you stumble?

The Fasiq's Scales Are Heavy with Faith
Refutes opponent's use of ayah about light scales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a fasiq's iman makes his scales heavy, countering the claim that they are light due to sins.

Core Idea
Faith outweighs sin; the fasiq's scales are heavy with iman.

In this lesson, we examine a proof used against the Ahl al-Sunna regarding the fate of the fasiq (major sinner). Opponents cited a verse about light scales to argue that the fasiq is doomed. Imam al-Baqillani refutes this by affirming that the fasiq's scales are heavy—not light—because of his faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
Begin by reading the opponent's citation of the Qur'anic verse about those whose scales are light, and their application to the fasiq.
The verse is from Surah al-Jathiya (45:31).
2
Understand Baqillani's response
Notice the concise refutation: 'بل ثقلت بالإيمان' (Rather, they are heavy with faith). This is the key statement.
3
Reflect on the theological point
Consider why this refutation matters: it defends that iman remains beneficial even with major sins, and that the ultimate judgment involves both iman and sins.
This relates to the Sunni position that faith is not nullified by sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: وقوله تعالى في حق من خفت موازينه: ألم تكن آياتي تتلى عليكم فكنتم بها تكذبون) والفاسق ممن خفت موازينه قلنا: بل ثقلت بالإيمان.
The sixth: His saying, exalted is He, regarding those whose scales are light: 'Were not My verses recited to you, and you used to deny them?' And the fasiq is among those whose scales are light. We said: Rather, they are heavy with faith.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of a proof used by opponents who cite the Qur'anic verse that those whose scales are light will be punished. They argue that the fasiq (major sinner) is among them. The author (Baqillani) responds that the fasiq's scales are heavy with faith (iman), not light, because he possesses iman which outweighs his sins. This guards against the idea that the fasiq's sins alone determine his fate.

Key Terms
الفاسق
خفت موازينه
ثقلت بالإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fasiq's scales are light because of his sins, so he is doomed regardless of faith.
Correction: The fasiq's scales are heavy because his iman outweighs his sins; faith is not nullified by major sins.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the argument that the fasiq's scales are light?
A) He agrees that the fasiq's scales are light.
B) He says the fasiq's scales are heavy because of his iman.
C) He says the fasiq has no scales.
D) He says the argument is invalid because the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq's iman makes his scales heavy, so he is not simply condemned; faith remains even with major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you believe your faith still outweighs your sins, or do you despair of Allah's mercy? This lesson says your scales are heavy with faith—will you let that truth pull you back to Allah, or will you continue to let sin make you feel lighter?
?
If the fasiq's scales are heavy with faith, what does that mean for your own balance? Are you willing to trust that your iman, however weak, is enough to tip the scales toward hope, and will you act on that hope today?

Blackened Faces: Apostates, Not All Sinners
Refutes that all fasiqs have black faces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani restricts the verse about blackened faces on Judgment Day to apostates, not all major sinners.

Core Idea
The verse on black faces targets apostates, not every fasiq.

The opponent uses the verse about blackened faces to argue that all fasiqs will be humiliated on Judgment Day. But Imam Baqillani carefully limits this to a specific group—those who disbelieved after believing. Let's see how he uses the Quran itself to narrow the meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and literal translation to understand the flow of the argument. Note the verse cited and the refutation.
2
Identify the opponent’s claim
The opponent claims that the verse shows fasiqs have black faces. Notice how the author responds by saying 'we do not concede that every fasiq is like that.'
3
See the author’s precise restriction
The author restricts the verse to 'some disbelievers' (ba‘d al-kuffar) based on the phrase 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?' — meaning apostates only.
This is a common tafsir principle: the specific occasion of revelation limits the ruling.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Why does this matter? If all major sinners had black faces, it would conflate sin with disbelief. The author protects the distinction between fisq and kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع: وقوله تعالى: يوم تبيض وجوه وتسود وجوه والفاسق ممن وجهه مسود. قلنا لا نسلم أن كل فاسق كذلك بل هي واردة في بعض الكفار لقوله: أكفرتم بعد إيمانكم)
The seventh: His saying, exalted is He: 'On the Day when faces become white and faces become black.' And the fasiq is among those whose face is black. We said: We do not concede that every fasiq is like that; rather it is stated regarding some disbelievers because of His saying: 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
The opponent uses the verse that on Judgment Day some faces are black, and claims the fasiq is among them. The author refutes by saying we do not accept that every fasiq has a black face; rather the verse refers specifically to those who disbelieved after having believed (apostates). This limits the application and prevents generalizing to all fasiqs.

Key Terms
تبيض وجوه
تسود وجوه
الفاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All major sinners (fasiqs) will have black faces on Judgment Day.
Correction: The verse about blackened faces specifically refers to apostates who disbelieved after having faith, as indicated by the Quranic phrase 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?'

Checkpoint
According to the author, to whom does the verse about blackened faces apply?
A) All major sinners (fasiqs).
B) Only apostates who disbelieved after faith.
C) All disbelievers.
D) Polytheists only.

Key Takeaway
Imam Baqillani limits the Quranic threat of black faces to apostates, preserving the distinction between sin and disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a Quranic verse to judge others without considering its context? How will you change that after seeing this refutation? Commit now to always examine context before applying a verse.
?
When you hear threats like 'faces become black', do you assume it applies to yourself or to every sinner? Are you willing to accept that the severity may be less than you thought, and repent sincerely rather than despair?
?
This lesson shows that not every sin leads to blackness. Does that make you complacent about your own sins, or does it drive you to distinguish between sin and disbelief? What specific sin will you work to abandon today?

Refutation: The Fasiq is Not Among the Companions of the Left
Refutes that fasiqs are companions of the left hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic description of the 'companions of the left hand' does not apply to all sinners, but specifically to those who disbelieve and deny Allah's verses.

Core Idea
The 'companions of the left' are deniers, not just any sinner.

The opponent argues that a fasiq (grave sinner) must be among the 'companions of the left hand' (ashab al-mash'amah) cited in the Quran. But the author demonstrates a logical flaw: the verse ties that status specifically to those who disbelieve and deny the verses. Fornicators and thieves are grave sinners, yet they do not necessarily deny the truth. Thus the verse cannot include all fasiqs. This lesson examines the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the opponent's claim and the author's objection.
Pay attention to the concise structure: claim, verse, counterexample.
2
Identify the opponent's proof
The opponent uses the verse 'And those who disbelieve in Our verses are the companions of the left hand' to classify all fasiqs as companions of the left.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author points out that fornicators and thieves commit major sins but do not deny the truth. Therefore the verse's condition (disbelief and denial) is not met, so the analogy fails.
The term 'yantaqida' means 'is contradicted by'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that the verse refers specifically to those who disbelieve and deny Allah's signs, not to all sinners. This refines the concept of 'companions of the left'.
This shows the importance of precise textual interpretation in kalam.

Source Text

The eighth: That he is among the companions of the left hand, and He says: 'And those who disbelieve in Our verses are the companions of the left hand.' This is contradicted by the fornicator and the thief, along with their not denying [the truth].

Commentary
The opponent claims the fasiq is one of the 'companions of the left hand' (ashābu al-mash'amah), citing the Qur'an that associates them with disbelief. The author objects that this is contradicted (yantaqida) by the cases of the fornicator (zānī) and the thief (sāriq), who commit major sins but do not deny (tukdhib) the truth. Thus the verse applies only to disbelievers who deny the verses, not to all fasiqs.

The term 'Fasiq' is not restricted to apostates
Refutes the argument that the verse restricts 'fasiq' to disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic verse 'those who disbelieve after that are the fasiqs' does not restrict the term 'fasiq' to apostates only.

Core Idea
Original disbelievers are also fasiqs because disbelief is the greatest sin.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani refutes a proof based on a Qur'anic verse. The opponent claimed the verse limits the word 'fasiq' to those who disbelieve after faith. The Imam counters by showing that original disbelievers commit the greatest sin, so the term applies to them as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the key terms: الكافر, الفاسقون, الكفر, الكبائر.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and the author's response
The opponent cites the verse and uses a linguistic principle (restriction of subject in predicate). The author denies this restriction. Find the words 'ممنوع' and 'قلنا'.
The author's response begins with 'قلنا' (we said).
3
Understand why the response works
The author says the original disbeliever is also a fasiq because he committed the greatest major sin: disbelief. Therefore, the verse does not exclusively describe apostates. Ask yourself: why is disbelief considered the greatest sin?
This connects to the broader idea that any sin, especially the greatest, makes one a fasiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع: ومن كفر بعد ذلك فأولئك الفاسقون) وأنه يقتضي حصر المبتدأ في الخبر قلنا. ممنوع لأن الكافر ابتداء كذلك. معناه أن الكافر الأصلي ابتداءًا هو فاسق حيث إنه ارتكب أكبر الكبائر وهو الكفر فلا وجه لحصر المعنى في فاعل الكبيرة عندهم.

So there is no way to restrict the meaning to the committer of a major sin according to them.

Commentary
The opponent cites the verse that says 'those who disbelieve after that are the fasiqs', claiming it restricts (yahsur) the term fasiq to those who disbelieve after faith. The author responds that this is not acceptable, because the original disbeliever (al-kāfir ibtidā'an) is also described as a fasiq, since disbelief itself is the greatest major sin (akbar al-kabā'ir). Therefore, the verse does not limit the term only to apostates; it simply identifies a subset of fasiqs.

Key Terms

الكفر

Misconception: The verse 'those who disbelieve after that are the fasiqs' implies that the word 'fasiq' in the Qur'an refers only to apostates, not to original disbelievers.
Correction: Original disbelievers are also fasiqs because disbelief is the greatest sin; thus the verse does not restrict the term to apostates.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The term 'fasiq' in the Qur'an includes both apostates and original disbelievers because disbelief itself is the greatest of major sins.

Refutation: The Fasiq Does Not Despair of God's Mercy
Refutes that the fasiq despairs of God's mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the claim that a fasiq necessarily despairs of God's mercy, thus preserving the distinction between sinful believers and disbelievers.

Core Idea
Sin does not equal despair; hope can coexist with sin.

This lesson addresses a key argument from opponents who claimed that a fasiq (sinful Muslim) must be a disbeliever because he despairs of God's mercy. The verse 'No one despairs of the Spirit of Allah except the disbelieving people' is used to support this. Imam Baqillani counters by affirming that the fasiq can still have hope (rajā'), so the verse does not apply to him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a concise refutation: the opponent quotes a verse and applies it to the fasiq, and the author denies it with the word 'mamnū‘' (denied) and gives the reason 'because of hope'.
The term 'rajā’' is key: it means hope or expectation of mercy.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues: The Qur'an says only disbelievers despair of God's mercy. The fasiq (by sinning) despairs of mercy. Therefore the fasiq is a disbeliever. Understand this logical chain.
The verse referenced is Qur'an 12:87.
3
Understand the author's response
Imam Baqillani denies the premise that the fasiq despairs. He asserts that the fasiq can still have hope (rajā') in God's mercy. Thus the verse does not apply to him, and the argument fails.
This response shows that sin and hope can coexist; despair is not a necessary consequence of sin.

Source Text

Commentary
The opponent uses the verse that only disbelievers despair of God's mercy, and claims the fasiq despairs (because he sins and expects punishment). The author denies this, stating that the fasiq can have hope (rajā') in God's mercy. Thus, the fasiq is not necessarily a disbeliever.

Checkpoint

Refuting the Eleventh Proof: Entering Fire and Disgrace
Refutes that entering Fire means disgrace, which is only for disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the claim that entering the Fire necessarily means complete disgrace, and how the definite noun 'al-' does not imply generality.

Core Idea
A definite noun does not always mean 'all' — context matters in kalam proofs.

In this lesson, we examine the eleventh proof raised by opponents who try to equate the fasiq with the disbeliever because both may enter the Fire. Imam Baqillani presents two key responses: one linguistic (the definite article does not imply universality) and one theological (disgrace can be complete or partial). By understanding this refutation, you will see how careful attention to Arabic grammar and theological nuance defends the distinction between fisq and kufr.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
Read the two verses the opponent combines: 'Whoever You admit to the Fire – You have disgraced him' and 'Indeed, the disgrace today and the evil are upon the disbelievers.' Notice how they argue that if the fasiq enters the Fire, he is disgraced, and disgrace is only for disbelievers.
The opponent thinks entering Fire always implies the same disgrace.
2
Understand the linguistic response
Imam Baqillani first responds that a definite noun (al-mufrad al-muḥallā bi al-lām) does not indicate generality. The word 'al-khizy' in the second verse may refer to a specific instance, not all instances of disgrace.
This is a key linguistic principle in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.
3
Understand the theological response
Alternatively, the word 'al-khizy' in the second verse could mean complete disgrace, which applies only to disbelievers. The fasiq may experience a lesser form of disgrace.
This preserves the distinction between the fasiq and the disbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر: إنك من تدخل النار فقد أخزيته) مع قوله: إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين قلنا: المفرد المحلى باللام لا عموم له، أو المراد به الخزي الكامل.
The eleventh: 'Indeed, whoever You admit to the Fire – You have disgraced him,' along with His saying: 'Indeed, the disgrace today and the evil are upon the disbelievers.' We said: The definite noun with the article does not indicate generality, or what is meant is complete disgrace.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
The opponent combines two verses: one saying that those admitted to the Fire are disgraced, and another stating that disgrace is specifically upon disbelievers. They argue that if the fasiq enters the Fire, he is disgraced, but disgrace is only for disbelievers, so he must be a disbeliever. The author responds by noting that a definite noun (al-mufrad al-muḥallā bi al-lām) does not imply generality; it could refer to a specific instance. Alternatively, the word 'disgrace' in the second verse might mean complete disgrace, which applies only to disbelievers, while the fasiq may experience a lesser form of disgrace.

Key Terms
الخزي
اللام
عموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definite article 'al-' in Qur'anic contexts always indicates universal application.
Correction: In Arabic, a definite noun without additional context can refer to a specific instance, not necessarily all instances. Thus, 'al-khizy' in the verse may refer to complete disgrace specific to disbelievers.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the argument that the fasiq must be a disbeliever because entering the Fire implies disgrace?
A) By denying that the fasiq enters the Fire.
B) By arguing that the definite noun 'al-khizy' does not necessarily imply generality.
C) By saying the verses are abrogated.
D) By claiming the fasiq is not disgraced.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq is not a disbeliever because entering the Fire can involve partial disgrace, while complete disgrace is reserved for disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew that even a lesser disgrace in the Fire is unbearable, what sin are you clinging to that you have not repented from? How does knowing that there are degrees of disgrace motivate you to avoid the smaller sins?
?
The disbelievers receive complete disgrace. But you, as a believer, might still face partial disgrace for your sins. Does this thought terrify you enough to change your daily actions? What is one habit you will abandon tonight after this lesson?
?
The opponent used a flawed interpretation to justify sin. Are you using any excuse to downplay the consequences of your own sins? Have you ever thought 'it's just a small sin' while forgetting that every sin brings some disgrace? Write down one specific sin you will genuinely strive to leave for the sake of avoiding even partial disgrace.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on the Status of the Fasiq
Refutes the Mu'tazila position that the fasiq is in an intermediate position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazila argument that the fasiq is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and why this is actually a refutation of the Khawarij.

Core Idea
The fasiq is a believer, not an intermediate.

The Mu'tazila argued that a major sinner (fasiq) cannot be called a believer nor a disbeliever, citing their own legal practices as evidence. Imam al-Baqillani meets this argument head-on by affirming that the fasiq remains a believer, and reframes the entire debate as a refutation of the Khawarij. This lesson walks through the Mu'tazila claim and the decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazila argument, then the author's reply.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
Identify the two premises: the fasiq is not a believer (according to their earlier reasoning) and not a disbeliever (because they apply Islamic law and bury him in Muslim cemeteries).
Their evidence from practice is strong, but the author will turn it against them.
3
Grasp the author's response
The author says simply 'He is a believer' and then reinterprets the whole discussion as a refutation of the Khawarij, who declare sinners disbelievers.
This shows that the Mu'tazila's own actions contradict their theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة: أن الفاسق ليس مؤمناً لما مر ولا كافراً بالإجماع لأنهم كانوا يقيمون عليه الحد ولا يقتلونه ولا يحكمون بردته ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين قلنا: هو مؤمن وتقدير الكلام فيه في الرد على الخوارج.
And the Mu'tazila argued: That the fasiq is not a believer according to what has passed, nor a disbeliever by consensus, because they used to apply the prescribed punishment (ḥadd) on him, they did not kill him, they did not rule his apostasy, and they buried him in Muslim cemeteries. We said: He is a believer, and the import of the speech is in refutation of the Khawarij.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument that the fasiq (major sinner) is neither a believer (because he sins) nor a disbeliever (because they apply Islamic penal law to him, do not kill him as an apostate, and bury him in Muslim graveyards). Thus they conclude an intermediate position. The author (Baqillani) responds simply: 'He is a believer,' and notes that the entire discussion is actually a refutation of the Khawarij who declare sinners disbelievers. The Mu'tazila's own practices show they treat him as a Muslim, which implies he is a believer (with deficient faith).

Key Terms

الرد على الخوارج

The fasiq remains a believer, and the Mu'tazila's own legal practices confirm this, not an intermediate state.

Why Sin Does Not Contradict Faith
Baqillani proves fisq and iman are not contradictory by locus difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational principle that fisq (sinful action) and iman (faith) are not contradictory because they belong to different loci: the limbs and the heart.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires the same locus; fisq and iman are in different domains.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani addresses a critical question: how can a person commit sins and still be a believer? He provides a clear rational proof based on the principle of contradiction. The key insight is that contradiction only occurs when two things occupy the same place - and fisq (actions of the limbs) and iman (belief of the heart) do not share a locus. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a general principle, an application, a proof example, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the sentence stating that two things are only contradictory if they are in one locus. Understand why this is the foundation of the argument.
The principle is: التضاد لا يكون إلا في محل واحد.
3
Examine the proof example
Look at the example about resolving to disobey the Prophet. See how the author uses it to show that a heart's resolution does not negate knowledge and affirmation.
The example demonstrates that a person can have both a sinful intention and true faith simultaneously.
4
Apply the argument to fisq and iman
Connect the example to the main claim: fisq (through limbs) and iman (in heart) are not contradictory. Reflect on why this refutes the idea that a sinner is not a believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : لأن الشيئين إنما يتضادّان في محلّ واحد. وقد علمنا أن ما يوجد بالجوارح لا يجوز أن ينفي علماً وتصديقاً يوجد بالقلب. فثبت أنه غير مضادّ للعلم بالله والتصديق له والدليل على ذلك أنه قد يعزم على معصية الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم بقلبه من لا ينفي عزمه على ذلك معرفة النبيّ صلّى الله عليه وآله وسلم، وتصديقه له وكذلك حكم القول في العزم على معصية الله عزّ وجلّ، وأنه غير مضادّ لمعرفته والعلم به والتصديق له هو الإيمان لا غير. فصح بذلك اجتماع الفسق الذي ليس بكفر مع الإيمان وأنهما غير متضادين
It was said to him: Because two things are only contradictory in one locus. And we know that what occurs through the limbs cannot negate knowledge and affirmation that occur in the heart. So it is established that it (fisq) is not contradictory to knowledge of Allah and affirmation of Him. The proof of that is that one may resolve in his heart to disobey the Messenger, peace be upon him, and that resolution does not negate his knowledge of the Prophet and his affirmation of him. Likewise the ruling concerning resolving to disobey Allah, Mighty and Majestic, and that it is not contradictory to knowledge of Him and cognizance of Him and affirmation of Him, which is faith and nothing else. Thus it is established that fisq that is not disbelief can coexist with iman, and that they are not contradictory.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) that fisq (sinful action) does not contradict iman (faith). The argument is based on the principle that two things are only contradictory if they exist in the same locus (maḥall). Since fisq occurs through the limbs (jawāriḥ) and iman is a matter of the heart (qalb) – knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (taṣdīq) – they are in different loci and thus cannot negate each other. The author gives an example: a person may resolve (ya'zimu) in his heart to disobey the Prophet, yet that resolution does not negate his knowledge and affirmation of the Prophet. Similarly, resolving to disobey God does not contradict one's knowledge of God and affirmation of Him, which is faith. Therefore, iman and fisq can coexist, and the fasiq is still a believer.

Key Terms
التضاد
محل
الجوارح
القلب
العزم
المعرفة
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any sin (fisq) completely negates faith (iman), making the person a non-believer.
Correction: The passage shows that fisq does not negate iman because they are in different loci: fisq is an action of the limbs, iman is knowledge and affirmation in the heart. A person can have both true faith and commit sins without losing faith.

Checkpoint
What is the basic principle used to prove that fisq does not contradict iman?
A) That iman is stronger than fisq.
B) That two things are only contradictory if they are in the same locus.
C) That fisq is not a sin.
D) That the limbs and heart are independent.

Key Takeaway
Fisq (sin) and iman (faith) can coexist because they occur in different loci: the limbs and the heart, respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered a sin you committed as proof that your faith is weak or nonexistent? How does this lesson's distinction between heart and body challenge that? What will you do the next time you sin to remind yourself that iman remains in your heart?
?
If faith is primarily in the heart, how honest are you about the state of your heart? Do you spend more effort polishing your outward actions or nurturing your inner conviction? Which one truly sustains your relationship with Allah?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim sinning, do you immediately doubt their faith? How will you apply Baqillani's argument to change your perspective? What can you do to support them without judging their iman?

The Linguistic Argument: Calling the Fasiq a Believer
Linguistic proof that a fasiq is called a believer due to existing faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic basis for calling a fasiq a believer despite his sin.

Core Idea
Names in Arabic are derived from the presence of their attributes, not their absence.

Imam al-Baqillani presents a linguistic argument to defend the position that a fasiq (sinful Muslim) must still be called a believer. The opponent asks: why call him a believer when he commits sins? Al-Baqillani answers by appealing to the rules of Arabic derivation (ishtiqāq). This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is in the form of an objection and answer.
2
Identify the linguistic principle
The author states that language experts derive names based on the existence of the attribute. Find this statement and understand its significance.
3
Apply the principle to both iman and fisq
See how the author applies the same reasoning to both 'believer' and 'sinner'—each name is derived from the presence of its respective attribute.
The two descriptions coexist without negating each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : ولم قلتم إنه يجب أن يُسمَّى الفاسق الملي بما فيه من الإيمان مؤمناً؟ . قيل له : لأن أهل اللغة إنما يشتقون هذا الاسم للمسمى به من وجود الإيمان به. فلما كان الإيمان موجوداً بالفاسق وصفنا حاله، وجب أن يُسمَّى مؤمناً، كما أنه لما لم يضاد ما فيه من الإيمان فسقه الذي ليس بكفر ، وجب أن يُسمَّى به فاسقاً. وأهل اللغة متفقون على أن اجتماع الوصفين المختلفين لا يوجب منع اشتقاق الأسماء منهما ومن أحدهما فوجب بذلك ما قلناه
If he says: Why do you say that the fasiq who belongs to the community (al-millī) must be called a believer because of the faith that is in him? It was said to him: Because the people of language only derive this name for the named from the existence of faith in him. So when faith exists in the fasiq, by describing his state, it is necessary that he be called a believer, just as, since what is in him of faith is not contradicted by his fisq that is not disbelief, it is necessary that he be called a fasiq because of it. And the people of language agree that the conjunction of two different descriptions does not necessitate preventing the derivation of names from both of them or from one of them. Thus what we said is necessitated.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic (lughawī) argument for calling the fasiq a believer. The opponent asks why the fasiq must be called a believer. The author answers that language experts only apply a name based on the presence of its attribute. Since iman exists in the fasiq, he should be called a believer (mu'min). Similarly, because his fisq does not negate his iman, he is also called a fasiq. The point is that the presence of two different attributes (iman and fisq) does not prevent naming from each attribute separately. Therefore, the fasiq is both a believer (due to his iman) and a fasiq (due to his sin). This refutes the view that he should only be called a fasiq, not a believer.

Key Terms
اللغة
الاشتقاق
الفاسق الملي
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person cannot be described by two opposing names like believer and sinner.
Correction: The passage shows that language allows multiple descriptions; the fasiq is a believer due to faith and a sinner due to sin; they coexist.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why must the fasiq be called a believer linguistically?
A) Because he has some good deeds.
B) Because the name is derived from the existence of faith in him.
C) Because he is a Muslim.
D) Because the Prophet commanded it.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq is called a believer because the name is derived from the existence of faith, which he still possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own sins, do you still consider yourself a believer because your faith exists, or do you let your sins define you entirely? What would change if you truly internalized that the name 'believer' is derived from the faith that is still present in you, not removed by your shortcomings?
?
If someone were to describe you based on your worst sin, would you accept that description as your identity? This lesson shows that two descriptions can coexist. How does that challenge the way you judge others who sin? Are you quick to label them only by their sin, forgetting the faith that remains?
?
Consider a time you felt your faith was weakened by sin. Did you still call yourself a believer? This lesson grounds that practice in a linguistic and theological principle. How does this principle affect your sense of accountability and hope? Write a commitment to remind yourself that your faith exists even amidst your struggles.

Sin and Faith: Why They Coexist
Baqillani proves that sin not kufr does not contradict iman using intention to disobey the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sin that is not unbelief does not contradict faith, based on the principle of differing loci.

Core Idea
Sin and faith are not opposites because they occupy different loci.

Imam al-Baqillani addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: does committing a sin negate one's faith? He argues that true opposition requires a single locus. Since sin occurs through the limbs while faith resides in the heart, they cannot be genuine opposites. This passage explains the reasoning and provides evidence from the intention to disobey the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow from principle to evidence to conclusion.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the statement that two things are only opposed in a single locus. Understand that this is the logical foundation of the argument.
Opposition (tadād) is a technical term in kalam.
3
Apply the principle
See how the author applies this: sin occurs through limbs, faith is in the heart. Different loci mean no true opposition.
4
Examine the evidence
Consider the example: one can intend to disobey the Prophet while still recognizing and affirming him. This shows that the resolve to sin does not negate faith in the heart.
This is a concrete proof that sin and faith coexist.
5
Conclude with the main takeaway
The passage concludes that sin not kufr coexists with iman. Reflect on why this matters for understanding the relationship between actions and faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : لأن الشيئين إنما يتضادّان في محلّ واحد. وقد علمنا أن ما يوجد بالجوارح لا يجوز أن ينفي علماً وتصديقاً يوجد بالقلب. فثبت أنه غير مضاد للعلم بالله والتصديق له والدليل على ذلك أنه قد يعزم على معصية الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم بقلبه من لا ينفي عزمه على ذلك معرفة النبيّ صلّى الله عليه وآله وسلم، وتصديقه له وكذلك حكم القول في العزم على معصية الله عزّ وجلّ، وأنه غير مضاد لمعرفته والعلم به والتصديق له هو الإيمان لا غير. فصح بذلك اجتماع الفسق الذي ليس بكفر مع الإيمان وأنهما غير متضادين
It was said to him: Because two things are only opposed to each other in a single locus. And we know that what occurs through the limbs cannot negate knowledge and affirmation that occur in the heart. So it is established that it is not opposed to knowledge of Allah and affirmation of Him. And the evidence for that is that one may resolve in his heart upon disobeying the Messenger (peace be upon him and his family) while that resolve does not negate his recognition of the Prophet (peace be upon him and his family) and his affirmation of him. And the same ruling applies to the resolve upon disobeying Allah Almighty, and that it is not opposed to recognition of Him, knowledge of Him, and affirmation of Him, which is faith and nothing else. Thus it is established that sin that is not unbelief coexists with faith and that they are not opposed.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a proof that sin which is not unbelief does not contradict faith. The author (Baqillani) begins by stating a fundamental principle: two things can only be true opposites when they exist in the same locus. He then applies this: actions of the limbs (where sin occurs) and faith (which is knowledge and affirmation in the heart) have different loci. Therefore, sin cannot negate faith. He provides evidence: a person can intend to disobey the Prophet in his heart while still recognizing and affirming the Prophet—so the intention does not negate the faith in the heart. Similarly for disobedience to Allah. The conclusion is that sin (fisq) that is not kufr can coexist with iman, and they are not mutually exclusive.

Key Terms
تضاد
محل
جوارح
قلب
علم
تصديق
فسق
كفر
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any sin contradicts faith, so a believer who sins loses their iman.
Correction: Only kufr (outward denial) contradicts iman; lesser sins (fisq) do not because they occur in a different locus (limbs) than faith (heart).

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does sin that is not kufr not negate iman?
A) Because sin is always less serious than faith.
B) Because sin and faith occur in different loci (limbs vs. heart).
C) Because Allah abolishes the sin of the believer.
D) Because the sinner does not intend to disobey Allah.

Key Takeaway
Sin that is not kufr does not negate iman because they occur in different loci.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that faith is in your heart, yet you commit sins repeatedly. Does this lesson make you complacent about those sins, or does it drive you to repent more urgently because you see that sin does not negate faith but still displeases the One you claim to love? What will you do today to turn back to Him?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that your faith is completely gone? How does knowing that sin and faith are not opposites (they have different loci) change how you view yourself after falling? What will you think next time you sin, and what action will you take to strengthen your heart's faith?
?
You may have looked down on a fellow Muslim who committed a major sin, assuming their faith was weak or absent. After this lesson, how must you now view that person? Will you separate their sinful act from their inner faith, and how will that affect your treatment of them? What accountability do you bear for judging them wrongly?

Naming the Sinner a Believer: Linguistic Derivation
Baqillani defends calling the sinner a believer based on linguistic practice of deriving names from existing attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that a Muslim sinner must still be called a believer based on the linguistic practice of deriving names from existing attributes.

Core Idea
Names follow attributes, not moral perfection.

This lesson examines Baqillani's response to the question of whether a sinner can be called a believer. He defends using the term 'believer' for sinners based on a simple linguistic principle: names are derived from attributes that are actually present. This clarifies an important point about faith and language.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Identify the question posed by the opponent and Baqillani's core response. The objection asks why the sinner must be called a believer, and the answer appeals to linguistic practice.
2
Understand the linguistic principle
Notice that Baqillani says language speakers derive names from the attribute that exists in the person. Since faith exists in the sinner, the name 'believer' applies.
This principle is key to the argument.
3
See the implication of coexisting attributes
Baqillani adds that sin and faith can coexist without preventing naming from either attribute, as agreed by language scholars. This shows that calling someone a believer does not deny their sin.
The conclusion is based on linguistic consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : ولم قلتم إنه يجب أن يُسمَّى الفاسق الملي بما فيه من الإيمان مؤمناً؟ قيل له : لأن أهل اللغة إنما يشتقون هذا الاسم للمسمى به من الذي وجود الإيمان به. فلما كان الإيمان موجوداً بالفاسق وصفنا حاله، وجب أن يُسمَّى مؤمناً ، كما أنه لما لم يضاد ما فيه من الإيمان فسقه الذي ليس بكفر ، وجب أن يُسمّى به فاسقاً. وأهل اللغة متفقون على أن اجتماع الوصفين المختلفين لا يوجب منع اشتقاق الأسماء منهما ومن أحدهما فوجب بذلك ما قلناه
Then he said: And why do you say that it is necessary that the Muslim sinner, due to the faith that is in him, be called a believer? It was said to him: Because the people of language only derive this name for the one named from that which the existence of faith is in him. So since faith exists in the sinner, we describe his state, and it is necessary that he be called a believer, just as, since his sin that is not unbelief does not oppose the faith in him, it is necessary that he be called a sinner by it. And the people of language agree that the coexistence of two different descriptions does not necessitate preventing the derivation of names from both of them or from one of them, so what we have said is necessitated.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer about the linguistic grounds for calling the sinner a believer. The opponent asks: Why must we call the Muslim sinner a believer because of the faith in him? The author answers by appealing to the practice of Arabic language speakers: names are derived from the attribute that is present in the person. Since faith is present in the sinner, we describe his state accordingly and thus he is called a believer. Similarly, because his sin does not oppose his faith, he is also called a sinner. The author notes that language scholars agree that two different attributes can coexist without preventing the derivation of names from either or both. Therefore, the conclusion stands.

Key Terms
فاسق ملي
إيمان
اشتقاق
اسم
لغة
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that calling a sinner a believer condones sin or denies the gravity of sin.
Correction: Baqillani is only making a linguistic point: names are derived from existing attributes. Calling someone a believer does not imply moral approval of their sin; it simply reflects the presence of faith.

Checkpoint
What is the linguistic principle used by Baqillani to justify calling the sinner a believer?
A) The name 'mu'min' is only for those who never sin.
B) Names are derived from the attributes that exist in the person.
C) The sinner is not really a believer but is called one out of courtesy.
D) The Sharia changed the meaning of the word.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that linguistic derivation from existing attributes requires calling the sinner a believer, without denying his sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you still name yourself a believer? Or have you let your failures define you more than your faith? Baqillani says names follow present attributes. Is your faith truly present? If not, what must you do today to ensure it is?
?
You have two attributes: your faith and your sin. Which one are you feeding? And which one do others see? Baqillani says both can coexist. But does your conscience accept both? What would change if you truly believed Allah sees both but still calls you a believer?
?
The passage argues from how language works. But what about your heart? Does calling yourself a believer while sinning feel like hypocrisy to you? How can you hold both your identity as a believer and your struggle with sin without falling into despair or arrogance? Commit to one specific change tonight.

Refuting the Claim of Sharia Changing Linguistic Names
Baqillani refutes the claim that Sharia transferred the names 'mu'min' and 'fasiq' from linguistic to legal meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's logical refutation of the claim that Sharia arbitrarily changed the linguistic meanings of 'mu'min' (believer) and 'fasiq' (sinner), and why such a claim leads to absurd consequences and contradicts the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Sharia does not arbitrarily rename; any such claim leads to self-defeating absurdity.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the position that the Sharia merely reaffirms linguistic meanings. The objector suggests that Allah might have changed the meanings to emphasize reward and punishment. Baqillani's response is both logical and grounded in Qur'anic usage, showing why such a claim cannot stand.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand what the objector is proposing: that Sharia transferred names from linguistic to legal meaning for the purpose of emphasizing reward and punishment.
Notice the objector's claim that the change is a 'sign' (علم) for deserving reward/punishment.
2
Analyze the reductio
Baqillani turns the tables by showing that if such arbitrary change were allowed, one could just as easily claim the opposite: that because Allah magnified faith, only the disbeliever could be called a sinner, which is false.
This is a classic argument by contradiction.
3
Connect to the earlier proof
The author references his earlier demonstration that changing names from linguistic origin is corrupt. Recall that Baqillani has already proven that Sharia affirms linguistic meanings, not alters them.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how this argument protects the integrity of linguistic meaning in religious texts and why arbitrary changes would contradict the Qur'anic usage.
This reinforces the principle that Sharia does not invent new definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فما أنكرتم أن يكون حكم اللغة ما ذكرتم غير أن الله تعالى عظم زجر الفاسق والمبالغة في عقوبته بأن حرمه التسمية بإيمانه وجعل تسمية المؤمن مؤمناً عَلَماً على استحقاقه ضرباً عظيماً من الثواب؟ وكذلك جعل تسمية الفاسق فاسقاً من أسماء الدين علماً لاستحقاقه ضرباً من العقاب العظيم، وأن يكون حكم هذه الأسماء في الشريعة منقولاً عن حكم اللغة؟ قيل له : هذه دعوى لا شبهة في سقوطها . ولو جاز لمدع أن يدعي ذلك، لجاز لآخر أن يدّعي أن الله تعالى لما عظم شأن الإيمان وبالغ في الفسق لما أراده الترغيب في فضله وجب سقوط التسمية بما قارنه من تغليب حكم الإيمان على الفسق وجعله مما يعلو ولا يعلى وقصد به إلى الدلالة على استحقاق الثواب. وهذا يوجب أن يكون الفاسق هو الكافر فقط، وأن مَن سواه فليس بفاسق ولا يُسمَّى بذلك. فإن لم يجب هذا، لم يجب ما قالوه ولأن في هذه الدعوى تصحيح تغير الأسماء عن طريقة اللغة ودفع ما تلوناه من التنزيل. وقد أبنا فساد ما يوجب ذلك من الأقاويل فيما قبل
Then a speaker said: But what do you deny that the ruling of the language is what you mentioned, except that Allah Almighty magnified the rebuke of the sinner and the emphasis on his punishment by prohibiting him from being named by his faith, and He made the naming of the believer as a believer a sign of his deserving a great type of reward? And likewise He made the naming of the sinner as a sinner from the names of religion a sign of his deserving a great type of punishment, and that the ruling of these names in the Sharia is transferred from the ruling of the language? It was said to him: This claim has no doubt about its collapse. And if it were permissible for a claimant to claim that, it would be permissible for another to claim that when Allah Almighty magnified the status of faith and emphasized sin because He desired encouragement in its virtue, it became necessary to drop the naming due to what accompanies it, by giving dominance to the ruling of faith over sin and making it that which rises and is not risen above, and intending by it to indicate deserving reward. And this would necessitate that the sinner is only the disbeliever, and that whoever is other than him is not a sinner and is not called by that. So if this is not necessary, what they said is not necessary. And because in this claim there is an affirmation of changing names from the way of language and a rejection of what we have recited from the Revelation. And we have already shown the corruption of the sayings that necessitate that in what preceded.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector suggests that the Sharia might have changed the linguistic meanings: Allah magnified the punishment of the sinner by prohibiting him from being called a believer, and made the name 'mu'min' a marker for great reward, and 'fasiq' a marker for great punishment, thus transferring the names from their linguistic to their legal senses. The author dismisses this as baseless. He then uses a reductio ad absurdum: if such a claim were allowed, someone else could claim that because Allah magnified faith, the name of faith should dominate over sin, leading to the conclusion that only the disbeliever is a sinner—which is false. Moreover, such a claim would require changing names from their linguistic origins and contradict the Qur'anic usage. The author asserts that he has already shown the corruption of such views earlier.

Key Terms
شريعة
لغة
نقل
تسمية
ثواب
عقاب
علَم
تنزيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Sharia can arbitrarily change the meanings of words to emphasize reward and punishment.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such a claim leads to logical absurdity and contradicts the Qur'anic usage of these terms.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue would happen if the Sharia had changed the linguistic names as the objector claims?
A) The sinner would be called a believer and the believer a sinner.
B) Only the disbeliever would be called a sinner, which is false.
C) The names would become obsolete.
D) The Qur'an would need to be reinterpreted.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the claim of Sharia altering linguistic meanings by showing it leads to absurdity and contradicts the Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a religious term like 'fasiq' or 'mu'min' is just a label for reward and punishment, forgetting that its linguistic root carries meaning? How might that disconnect affect your understanding of Allah’s words?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you consciously consider the original Arabic meanings of its terms, or do you rely on translations and settled definitions? What is at stake if you ignore the linguistic foundation?
?
Baqillani argues that making claims about Allah’s actions without evidence leads to absurdity. Is there an area of your faith where you hold an opinion about Allah’s wisdom without grounding it in revelation? Will you examine that today?
